After I have finished with you, my son, this book which is the last of my works on medicine, completed with its end in view, following the explanations and affirmations of this treatise, I wish to complete it for you with this treatise which is a part of the operation, that is, surgery with the hand.
Since surgery with the hand is now abandoned in our time and is a private matter: until perhaps its science is lost and its practice has ceased, except for brief notices in ancient books, which have been altered by the hand: and there have happened to be mistakes and hesitation, until its intentions have been closed and its purpose prolonged: I have deemed it necessary to provide this treatise with an orderly arrangement of its contents, according to its position and affirmations, and with brevity: and to include the forms of instruments, such as the Cautery and other implements, with their specifications and their preparation which is necessary
However, because a skilled artisan of this sort is not to be found easily in our time with his hand: for the art of medicine is long, and the author must exercise himself beforehand in the science of anatomy, as Galen has said, until he knows the functions of the limbs, their forms, and their connection, and separation, and has an understanding of the bones, nerves, and lacerations, and their number, and their exit, and the pulse of veins, and quietness, and the locations of their exits.
For this reason Hippocrates said, "A name is much more than a name, and work is little, especially in the art of the hand.
We have indeed mentioned this at the beginning of this book.
For one who is ignorant of what we have said concerning anatomy will fall into error, and will endanger human life: as I have seen many who have learned this science carelessly and without study or experiment
Why is it that the doctor has secured the scrofula in the woman's neck with a stiff ligature, and has thereby obstructed some of the arteries in the neck? Because of this, blood flows from the woman until she dies in her hands.
And the doctor observed another one who had presumed to extract a stone from the bladder which was in the bladder, and the stone was large. He went to him and extracted it with the bladder's husk Because the man died on the third day.
And I too was called to extract it: and I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who took hold of a certain Duke of our land because of medicine: and it happened to a black eunuch who was with him, that he fractured his leg near the heel: and the doctor, with his ignorance, bound the fracture with vinegar and nails, and tight bands, and did not let the wound breathe.
And he died according to his desires. Afterwards he sent him away for a few days, and gave him orders not to loosen the ligament: and he stood thus until the swelling of his leg had gone down, and his foot and leg, and came to an end
I was called to him again: and I set about loosening the ligament: and there ensued peace, and he was freed from his pains
But indeed the corruption was confirmed in the limb: and I could not restrain it
So the corruption ceased to spread in the limb, until it died
And the physician went to another, who had perforated the cancerous abscess: and it was ulcerated after days, until its malice was increased with the lord himself.
For when a cancer is pure from melancholic humour, it must not be treated with iron in every case, except in a limb which can tolerate it, that it be entirely excised
And therefore, my son, you must know that an operation is divided into two parts: that which is associated with health, and that which carries risk in various situations
I am grieved in every place of this book where an operation is mentioned, where fear and terror are present.
Therefore you must be cautious of this, and be restrained: lest they find fault with you, and speak dishonestly
Receive therefore with humility and protection, and the sick with facility and firmness, and use the better way that leads to greater glory to God concerning the preservation of life and the obtaining of praise: and banish the terrifying diseases which are difficult to cure: and take away from your soul that which makes you fear, so that there may be no obstacle to your faith and your comfort
For there remains for you a greater glory in your faith, and a sublimity in this world, and finally in the number of your souls.
For Galen has said in some of his admonitions. You do not heal a bad disease: do not speak of a bad physician.
I have indeed divided this treatise into three chapters
Chapter One.
Of cautery with fire, and of cautery with acute medicine, a chapter, and ordered from the head down to the feet, concerning the forms of instruments, and of materials for cautery: and everything which is necessary for the operation
Second Chapter.

But before any operation is performed, it is necessary to state the qualities of the bandages and instruments to be used: and in what complexion they should be displayed
I say therefore, that the discourse concerning the quality of the cautery instrument, and its name, is a lengthy, subtle, scientific, and occult discourse.
And it is said by many wise men in this matter, and they disagree among themselves: and indeed little is sufficient from their discourses, fearing the prolongation of the discussion
I say therefore, that the cautery instrument is useful in all complexions, which is with material, and without material: except for two complexions, which are the hot complexion without material, and the dry complexion without material
In the hot and dry complexion with material they disagree Some indeed say that the cautery instrument is useful in these. Others argue that Cauterium is not suitable for carbuncles, which arise from heat and dryness, because fire itself is hot: and it is unsuitable to cure a hot and dry ailment with heat and dryness.
Others maintain that Cauterium is not suitable for any hot and dry carbuncles that occur in the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of a man with fire and moisten it, you find the body of a man to be cold.
I too have heard from experience that this is true.</start_of_turn>
It is true that it does not suffice simply to say that Cauterium is not suitable for carbuncles; for one must have studied Cauterium in its final exercise and become acquainted with the diversity of human complexions, the nature of ailments, their causes and their course.</start_of_turn>
With the other complexions there is no fear; and especially with cold and moist ailments.</start_of_turn> I know that all physicians agree about this: and they do not deny the use of Cauterium in these matters.</start_of_turn>
And indeed, the sons know that the secret of Cauterium with fire and its advantage over Cauterium with burning medicine lies in the fact that fire is simple, and its action does not extend beyond the part which is cauterized: and it does not harm any other connected part, except for a slight bruising.</start_of_turn
Burning medicine, however, extends its action to the parts surrounding the cauterized area: and sometimes it makes the healing of the ailment more difficult, and sometimes it even brings about death.</start_of_turn>
Fire, however, because of its subtlety and the excellence of its substance, does not make this [surgery] ill, unless it be excessive.
Fire, however, by reason of its subtlety and the excellence of its substance, does not make this [surgery] bad, unless it be excessive.
Fire, however, by reason of its subtlety and the excellence of its substance, does not make this [surgery] bad, unless it be excessive. And although this is not mentioned in this book, I shall tell you a secret concerning fire which is useful for this operation and which is demonstrated by philosophers with reference to the expulsion of ailments from the body.
And behold, children, that even they [philosophers] disagree concerning the time when fire becomes a cautery, and they say that it is better to delay the time of the operation.
I say also that cautery is useful at all times: for in every case the document becomes submerged in urine, which is attracted by the cautery. Above all, if the cautery is to be used for severe and sudden pains, which do not tolerate delay, for these are subject to the danger of an increase in evil which is greater than the slight damage done by the operation at a later time.
Do not be deceived by the minds of the vulgar or stupid physicians, who say that surgery is never necessary for ailments which are cured by cautery: for in this they are mistaken. Indeed, cautery alters the complexion and dries up humors which are the causes of these ailments.
Indeed, cautery surpasses medicine in speed of action, in the excellence of its operation, and in its gentleness: and it is possible that the ailment will be cured at some later hour according to the complexion of the patient, the location of the ailment, and the excellence of the operation itself. And this is effected in the body by the collection of superfluous humors which are evacuated by the cautery. And this is the reason why it brings about the healing of ailments which medicine cannot cure.
Acute pain in a few superabundant and humid parts, as in a burnt tooth, because of pain, and similar to it, is possible that it will not depart from there without pain. But this is in few cases.
Indeed, it is right that the cauterism is the most recent of the medical arts, and that is why they do not believe that there is any healing that can be obtained by medicine, nor by anything else after the cauterism has been made.
But the thing is contrary to that. The intention is not that the cauterism is the most recent of the medical arts, but when we administer methods of treatment in some ailment, and they do not confer that treatment: then we administer in the end the cauterism, and it confers: thus it is that the cauterism is the last thing, not according to the intention which the vulgar people and the multitude of the physicians understand
The ancients say that the cauterism with gold is better than the cauterism with iron: and they do not say this except because of the equality of the gold and the fineness of its substance: and they say that it does not form pus at the place of the cauterism But it is not absolutely so. For I myself have seen this to be the case, and I have found that it does not do this in some bodies without other things
And the cauterism with the very thing is good, and better than with iron, as they say. But when you ignite the cauterism with fire from gold, you are not to declare that it is ignited according to its quantity, because of the redness of the gold: and when it approaches the cold and if you add to it more fire, it liquefies in the fire, and turns into plates.
Why does the artificer depart from that to engage in this business? And because of this, the Cauterium with the iron is brought near and is more rapid and proximate to the operation from its upright position.
I have also ordered this Chapter in Cauterium, according to the Chapters: and I have ordered them from the head down to the feet: so that they may perform whatever they wish from it.
Chapter 2: Of the Head.
Here the cauterization is done according to the lord of humidity and cold over the brain: because these two are the causes of swelling, and of the flow of humors from the head to the eyes and ears, and of the swelling of sleep, and of toothache, and of throat pain: and in general of all diseases which arise from cold, such as palsy, and swelling, and epilepsy, and apoplexy, and things like these from diseases.
The method of cauterization is as follows: when the patient is in good health, he should be evacuated at first with a purgative medicine which softens the head for three or four nights, according to the nature of the disease, and the age of the patient, and his habit.
Then command him to make a circular burn on his head with the lancet Then make him sit between your hands square, so that you can place your hands on his chest
Then place the root of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes Wherever your middle finger touches, that is the place where you must cauterize with the burn
Then take the Cauterium Olive: which has this shape
Then place it upon the marked place with the cautery, descending gradually, so that your hand may be gradually constricted, and you shall withdraw it. Then draw your hand quickly, and grasp the place.

Then take some salt and dissolve it in water, and drink it down with this sponge, and place it upon the place: and keep it there for three days.
Then place upon it a sponge soaked in butter, and keep the flame over the place, until the eschar is consumed by fire. Then care for it with unguents of four herbs, until it is healed
It has been said that the greater the wound remains open, the more pus will be emitted: which is better and more useful.

Of the cauterization of the flux of blood from the artery in the wound.
Many times blood from an artery escapes from an incision made in the wound, which happens either externally, or at the base of the wound, or from the cautery of a limb, or like this: and this makes it difficult to close the wound
When this happens to anyone, then take hold of your hand with your thumb over the orifice of the artery, and place your index finger over that: and tighten it much, until blood ceases to flow from under your finger, and does not escape from there again
Then put on the fire a few small olive stones and many more in great number: and blow over them until they are kindled.
Then take one, or a few, or many bones, according to the wound and place, where the artery is ruptured, and put the cautery on the very part: then move your finger quickly: and hold the cautery until the blood is severed.
If it is expelled with the finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then take another cautery quickly, from those which are prepared in the fire
Do not cease to do this until the blood is severed.
And be careful not to burn the nerve which is there, and do not add any other evil to the sick person
You know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to sever it: especially when the artery is great, except with four fingers together Or with the cautery, as we have said Or with extraction, when it is not extracted For when it is extracted, its extremities contract and the blood is severed Or it is bound with a strong ligature. Or there are placed upon it medicines which have the property of severing blood, and they are bound with a constriction of thread.
Whoever performs an excision with a ligature, and a tightening with a bandage, or position of the burning things, or like these, does not give him pain, except rarely.
If however this happens to anyone, and there is no physician or medicine present: then begin with the position of the index finger over the opening of the vein, as we have said, and the constriction of the hand, until the blood is overcome from the upper part of the wound, and over the artery itself And the finger must not be moved, but should be left resting upon the cold water until the blood congeals, and swells, and is severed.
Meantime, let him consider what is needed from the cautery, or from the medicine.

Of the care for water which accumulates in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
You know that this infirmity often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife tightens the head of the child without gentleness
Whenever anything happens even from an occult cause which is not discerned.
I am not aware of any such ailment among adults as I am among children; and concerning all those from whom I have learned, the sea has been their master: on account of this, therefore, I deem it expedient to omit the operation in this case.
I have observed among children that whenever their heads were filled with water and their heads grew large every day, they could not sit down due to the size of their heads, and there was an increase in moisture until they died.
And such moisture either accumulates between the skin and bones or accumulates under the bones on the surface of the wound
If there is moisture between the skin and bones and the swelling is slight, then it is necessary to make a single incision in the middle of the head, the length of which should be like two knots, until the moisture is drained out
This is the form of the spatula.
If there is much moisture and the swelling is great, then make two incisions according to this form.</start_of_turn>

Chapter V.
About the care of acute tears and catarrh of the eyes from the inside of the head.
When acute catarrh, acres, or assiduity occur to anyone: and the tears of his eyes are prolonged, small, and the sight is impaired: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from the edge of the eye, and there is acute pain in the depth of the head, groaning, and sneezing follows: then take from these signs that the matter and the catarrh do not come from one source, except from many tears.
And the best cure for the eye is this. And it is to make the patient scrape the front of his eye with his finger Then make an incision one-third in the middle of the front, or somewhat lower in breadth, and begin from the left side to the right side: and let the incision reach the bone: and stretch your hand from the bites of the teeth, which move during chewing: until the bone is detected: and you will extract all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with cotton wool or strips of linen. Then bind above with thread: and soak the thread with wine and oil, so that no abscess forms.
And when you have finished with this, and the swelling of the abscess has gone down: then it is necessary to heat the bone, until flesh begins to form in it.
And then care for the eye with the regimen of drying which makes flesh grow: like if you take from wheat flour two parts, and from colophony four parts: and prepare from these materials a plaster, and give it to the eye until the flesh grows in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
And concerning the care of the eye that falls in the autumn.
Every part that falls into gold is of four kinds: either a mineral lapis, or one like it, such as iron; or it is derived from a plant, such as cicer and beans; or it is a flowing substance, such as water and vinegar, and things that resemble it; or it is some animal.

Then put on the eye a liniment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and olive oil, and beeswax. Then bind the eye over the plaster with many small beads
And thus with the pouls [pullets] it is: take many fine threads and double every thread above four: make many from these: then put one over the other according to the quantity required for the eye: then tighten over these with a strong thread.
Repeat the process until the wound is closed without leaving a scar.
Then remove the sutures and keep the eye closed day and night.
If, however, the ligament is again softened, then it is necessary to tighten it much: then remove the ligament after a day and a night, as we have said
If therefore you again see swelling in the eye, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, the pouls, the tightening and the sutures until the eye is healed.
Of the incision of the tunica of the eye.
Chapter 21.
When a rupture occurs in the tunica of the eye, then it will protrude and proceed outside the eyelids like a grain of the tunica, and will be deformed like a man because of this. Then it is necessary to make an incision in this: and the operation in this is as follows: and it is that you introduce a needle into the root of the tunica from the lower part to the upper part.
Then introduce another needle in which there is a doubled thread from the lachrymal part, and make it penetrate: and remove the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the doubled thread and ligate with it the part of the tunica around the needle: and tighten it much
Then remove the needle, and put over the eye a lanolin bandage on an albumin: and leave it in place until the thread is gone with the tunica
Then care for the eyes while they are comforted, until the wound is healed.
And when the limb is bound up from every part with a circle of cloth, until the eye regains great elevation
Then pierce with a fine needle that reaches the depth of the eye, and let out the humidy of the albumen, and the eye will descend, and join immediately: then bind it up until it is healed.
On the treatment of the eye.
Chapter XXII.
This ailment which is called Alcomnati, is not except for health which collects in the eye, like descending water, and is not itself
And the way of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared seat, or directly
Then grasp his head with your hands from two parts, and move him until you see the health go up to the lower parts of your eyes Then it will remain fixed, and will not descend, and the light will be extinguished: and you will know that it is water
If however the preparation of the descent of the health is not done with it which we have said, then make him sit between your hands Then take a fine needle, and pierce with it above the corneal membrane near the continuation of the conjunctiva with the membrane of the cornea in a circle, until the health comes out.
When it comes out, then instill in the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have ground barley and honey: then care for it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed
Concerning the care of water which flows into the eye, or a cataract.
Concerning the care of water which flows into the eye, or a cataract.
We have already spoken of ten kinds of water which exist in the eye, and of which some are harmful and some are beneficial; take heed of this according to the state of health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you should keep the diseased hand away from the eye opposite the light, near the cheek: and bind the healthy eye tightly: then grasp the eyelid of the eye which has the water in it with your right hand, if the water is in the left eye: or grasp the eyelid of the right eye if the water is in the left eye
Then grasp your right hand with the almond, if the eye is left: or your left hand if the eye is right.
Then put the tip of the almond near the edge of the visible white part of the eye: then press the almond with force: and you will see that the almond has penetrated the membrane of the eye. Feel that the almond has reached its endpoint
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the almond be to the depth, the measure of the space from the pupil to the end of the black part: and that is the circumference of the eye You will see this in the pupil of the eye, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then put the almond until it reaches the water Then press the lower eyelid downwards one after the other.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the diseased hand will immediately close over what has opened its eye, and the almond in the eye.

And remain quiet according to your custom until the third day.

When the diverse meats of the nose are cut, something is absorbed from the numerous feet of scorpions: from this is a cancerous tumor, a stone, of saffron color; from this is what is a soft tumor, not of saffron color.

Of the suture of the nose, and the lip, and the ear

About the extraction of the knots of the lips.
Many people have happened to have swelling in the insides of their lips like hard seeds of grass: and some of them are minor, and some are major

Then it is to be bound with vinegar, and the place is to be cared for with that which has styptic power, until the wound is healed
Chapter XXVIII.</start_of_turn
About the incision of the newly-formed flesh upon the gum
Many times there is formed upon the gum new flesh which the ancients call abuluss
Therefore it is necessary to suspend the flesh with a hook: or to grasp the flesh with forceps, and cut it off at its root, and let the blood run, or the pus flow out
Then place upon the place some coarse sponge, or other powder which has styptic power.
If the flesh returns again after treatment, for it often returns many times, then cut through it a second time and burn it: for it will not return after it has been burned.

Regarding the wisdom of teeth with iron.

Chapter XXVIII.</start_of_turn
It is necessary that the tooth be freed from its pain with all possible skill, and afterwards its eradication be aimed at. It is not in its power to remove the pain without eradicating it: for it is a lofty substance. Therefore, when eradication is not indicated, it is necessary to consider that the sickly person must eradicate it, in order to strengthen himself, and to confirm to you that the tooth is painful
Many men indeed deceive the sickly person with their pain, and say that it is in the tooth itself and eradicate it: but the pain does not depart until the diseased tooth is eradicated.
We do not indeed go about this from the performance of blood-lettings.
When it is confirmed to you that the tooth itself is painful, then it is necessary to make incisions around the tooth with sponges, in which there is some advantage, until the gums are separated from all parts: then move the tooth with your fingers, or with fine forceps from the outset gradually, until you break it
Then place the forceps in a large place well, and hold the patient's head between your knees: which you have done well, for it will not move: then draw out the tooth in accordance with the rule, so that you do not break it
If it does not come out, then grasp any of those instruments, and introduce it under it from all parts with ease, and endeavor to move it, as you did at first.
If the tooth is perforated or corroded: then it is necessary to fill the hole with gauze: and tie it with the extremity of a fine thread, so that it does not break in the time of your constriction with the forceps.
And it is necessary that you take care when you make incisions around the gums from all parts much.
Be steadfast in your fortitude, lest you break your own spirit, and a part of it remain broken, and return upon the infirm from that malice which is greater than his initial pain.
Be cautious lest you do that which the unskilled do, phlebotomists in their frenzy, and overween their own eradication, besides that which we have mentioned
Many do indeed make their patients endure great illnesses, from which it is difficult to recover, and leave behind roots of their entire being, or extract some of them with teeth of the jaw: as we have often seen
Then it is joined after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
When blood flows out from any place (for many do indeed flow out from their limb), then there is something from the limb which remains: and put in with it the cautery. If not, cauterize it if it does not give you the limb back.
The tweezers which are used to grasp the teeth in the first place are long-handled, short-handed, thick, so that they do not bend when pressure is put upon them: they are like large nails, until when they are bound to the tooth, they do not bite into themselves, neither do they bend: short-handled, and made of iron or of hammered steel, direct-handed, and in their handles are teeth, some of which enter into some of the others, and bind them together loosely, firmly
And when the handles become like the beak of a bird which is called a ciconia, and are strong in their constriction.

Chapter 33.
On the return of the tooth with threads.
When it happens to the front teeth that concussion and displacement has occurred from a blow, or in the case: and the patient cannot hold the bite with his hand because of pain: and it is not useful to apply treatment for it: then it is necessary to know in the tooth itself that it must be bound with thread: for silver is easily tarnished, and putrefies after many days: but gold never changes
And let the middle thread be of moderate thickness and strength according to the quantity that it can grasp between the teeth.
And the form of the return is that you grasp the thread and pass the loop through between two sound teeth: then you tie with two ends of the thread between the teeth that are loose, whether one or more, until you have reached with the thread until the sound tooth of the other part And repeat the tying until you reach the part from which you began: and bind your hand with moderate skill and knowledge, so that it does not move forward: and let your constriction be such as to bind the thread at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not swell
Then insert the superabundant extremities with scissors: and gather each one together, and close them with the thumb: and hide them between the sound tooth and the moved tooth, so that it does not damage the tongue: then release it very tightly as it remains.
If, however, it is dissolved or fractured, string it with another thread, and keep it firm as for the whole time
This is the form of a tooth, and the figure of the alternation of two sound teeth and two moved teeth, as with water.
And when it is necessary, as a tooth returns or two after falling out in their places, and returns as we have said, and remains: and it does not happen except by a skillful, prudent, and experienced artist.
And when something is adapted from bones or teeth: and it is made like a tooth in shape, and is placed in the place from which the tooth is extracted and bound, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time
Chapter XXIII.
When this ligament under the tongue happens naturally, as when a man is born, or accidentally by an injury that has healed.

Chapter 5.
When it happens under the tongue a small swelling that is a ranula, preventing the tongue from its natural action: and perhaps it will grow so much that it fills the mouth

Then it is bound with vinegar and salt: and care for the rest of the treatment suitable for this, until it is healed.
For the treatment of an abscess of two amygdalar.
Chapter 6.
When these things happen between the guttur godad, that is, the glands which are like peas, which happen outside: and which are called two amygdalas: when you cure them with what we said in the previous discourse, and they do not heal, then consider them
If there is a hard abscess, of a brown colour, with little sensation: do not touch it with iron
And if it is red in colour, and its root is large: do not touch it even with iron, fearing the flow of blood, rather let it go, until it matures And then you will either perforate it: or it will rupture by itself
And if it is white in colour, round, and its root is thin: then you must cut it open
And the operation in this is as follows: as you consider before your operation, if the abscess has become cold and quiet: then lie the patient with his head towards you: and open the abscess, and take out the pus between your hands, and pull down the tongue with an instrument of this shape
Do it with silver, or with air, which is subtle like a knife
When you have finished with the tongue, and you see the abscess, and it has gone down on your hand: then take pliers, and hold it in the abscess, and extract the pus to the outside as much as you can, without pulling out anything from the clothes.
Then strike the wound with the instrument whose form follows this. The instrument's tip should resemble that of scissors: its extremities are bent outwards so that the opening of each is opposite the other at an acute angle: hence care must be taken so that it does not slip or be imbued with alum.
If such an instrument is not available, then strike the wound with the spatula, whose form is similar to that of the instrument described above It should be acutely angled from one side, not from the other side
And after you have made an incision in one place, make an incision in the other place according to the same kind of incision.
Then let the patient gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If blood flows from the wound, make the patient gargle with water in which pomegranate seeds or myrtle leaves have been boiled: or similar substances like sponges, until the bleeding stops: then care for the wound until it has healed

Of the incision of the small apostema.
Chapter 7
When the catarrus descends to the lower part, and there is an apostema, and it is long: then it is called perpendicular: and if it is in the lower part large and round, then it is called a loop
When it is cured with that which we said in the explanation, and the cure does not suffice: and you see that the apostema is cold and quiet from it: then it is necessary to cut it off
And what is aggregated round about it, and is not of its length, and is bloody, or of the colour of rust, or black, and has no feeling: then it is necessary to make an incision of it In every ailment there is fear over the infirm.
Whenever you deal with an ailment, fear is proper when you do as I have said in white, and in length, so that you make the infirm sit before the sun: and touch his tongue with an instrument whose precedent I have mentioned: then fix the knot in one, and draw it downwards: and cut through it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the incision of two amygdales.
It is proper that you do not cut through from that which adds to the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut through from that which adds to the natural limit, you will make the wound and the speech worse
Then after the incision make use of that which we have narrated in the incision of two amygdales: and care for him, until he is healed.
If however the infirm person returns from the incision: then it is proper that you use your ingenuity in the cauterization of his without fear and aversion.
And the mode of cauterization in this is not except with acute water: and it is that you place the head of the infirm person in your lap. Then touch his tongue with the instrument which we mentioned. Then take from the water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with burnt lime, and put it on him not thick, neither thin, and make an incision from this with the help of this instrument
So that the extremities of it where the medicine is placed are of the shape of the cochlea of a radius
And place the instrument with the medicine on the thing itself: and the infirm person lies on his side, so that the moisture from his mouth comes out from the medicine from within, so that it does not go down into his gut and harm him
Then take your hand with the medicine. And you from the first part put it above the wound quantity of half an hour, until you see the wound has diminished, and then cease the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish to take hold of the limb, and cover it with the medicine itself over the end of the radius, and fill the limb with the medicine, and introduce the radius with the thumb into the cannula above, until the limb adheres to the wound over half an hour
Do it many times, until you follow that you wish for the cautery of the vein Then let go of the medicine: itself will dry and fall off in three days, or four.
And if you think it is necessary to go over the medicine, go over it: and after cauterization remove the surrounding area of the vein with the thumb, and moisten with butter, and bandage with the same as was in the surrounding of the medicine: and cool it with cold water.
And take care of the outside with sutures, and of the inside with gargling, until it is healed
Thus is this kind of treatment safer than an incision and longer lasting in fear
And when the wound is healed even with this medicine that is subtler than cautery and incision: it is healed by fumigation, according to this method. Take calamine, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and asa, and chamomile, and marjoram, and similar things from herbs: and gather them all together, or some of them in a pot, and immerse them in vinegar, and boil: and the pot should be covered with a lid: and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, above which is made a concave instrument according to this method.
Let air or silver issue forth: and let the extremity be introduced into that which contains the pomegranate, until the vapor rises up to the upper opening of the tube, until the upper part becomes dark much.
Then repeat over the much, until it softens.
And be careful, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of an attack of fever. Praise be to you: for in fever there is to be added in the bowels: and it does not be necessary to do this except when the fever has subsided
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take the tube and compound in its extremity bark or resin: that it does not burn the bone of the sick: for the bark of the resin hinders the vapor from burning the bone: and this is from a good treatment with health.
About the extraction of thorns and bones from the gullet.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Many things adhere to the gut: fish bones, or thorns, or something else.
It is necessary therefore that you extract what is seen to have fallen down from the gut, after you have moved the tongue with the instrument at the epiglottis, and declared that it is in the gut
And that which is not seen by you and is hidden in the gut, it is necessary that you make the sick vomit before food is digested in the stomach Perhaps therefore also the thing that is annexed will go out with the vomit.
Or the sick person may swallow a piece of poppy seed, or the root of lettuce, or swallow a dry piece of bread: or take a dry piece of sponge of the sea and bind it up, and then swallow it down When it has reached the place where the bones or spines are, or are extracted, it then departs.
If it does not depart with that which we have said: then use an instrument of lead in this fashion.
Let it be thicker than a small radius, and have a curvature at its end, which intrudes into its gut without difficulty: and let its head be elevated above itself.
Beware of its touch upon the epiglottis, so that it does not cause it to swell, and drive out with it the bone, or the spine, or be carried in by the physicianâ€™s hand itself
However that the patient intrudes it, that is better: because of his knowledge of the place of the thing annexed: and he drives downwards, or pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, the whole thing according to the quantity which is prepared for it, until it departs
Of the extraction of the blood-sucker
Chapter XXXIX.
When the physician has treated the blood-sucker with that which I have mentioned in the treatise on care with the physicians, and it does not yield, then consider in the belly of the patient at the sole or behind the tongue after you have drawn out its tongue with the instrument which I mentioned to you.
If therefore your instrument should fall upon the blood-sucker, draw it out with a small hook, or with a handle held loosely in the middle
If that is not possible with the hook: then take a curved needle, and introduce it into the belly of the patient up to near the gut: then introduce into the curve of the needle a burning iron with fire. Do this often: and let the patient abstain from all water for the whole day.
Then he shall take a full treatment of cold water and open its mouth in it: and he shall join his jaw with it and he shall not swallow anything from it: and he shall drink water an hour after the other with his hand. The blood-sucker will be almost finished when he feels the water falling.
But if he does not depart with it as we have said, then he must fumigate the patient with sponges, or with an instrument as I have mentioned in fumigation of the fistula.</start_of_turn>
Do this often, for it goes away by itself.</start_of_turn>
And the manner of operation in fumigation is that you take a vessel in which there are grapes of vitriol with fire: and the vessel is covered with a cover, in the middle of which is a hole: and you make an incision in that hole with the instrument.</start_of_turn
Then he throws away the fumigation and places the patient's jaw at the end of the tube, and closes his jaw, so that it does not escape the fumigation, until you know that the fumigation has passed to the blood-sucker.</start_of_turn For the blood-sucker immediately goes away.</start_of_turn>
And if it does not go away, then repeat the fumigation often.</start_of_turn>
And the patient can tolerate to remain sitting, and eat garlic and onions, and does not drink water.</start_of_turn
It is necessary therefore that he depart with this regimen.</start_of_turn
And this is the form of the instrument with which the blood-sucker is extracted from the gut when it falls upon the said vomit.</start_of_turn
It is like tweezers, thus: except that it has this curvature which enters the gut.</start_of_turn And its extremity is like the wing of an owl named ciconia, in which there is coarseness of the lime: when it grasps something, it does not relinquish it entirely.
And concerning the opening of abscesses, their section, and their care in general
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of various modes, according as the nature of the reminder of them is remembered: and they are diverse in their perforation and section into two modes.
One of them is the species of abscess which is from the moisture of the body itself.
The other is that which happens from the body itself
For indeed an abscess which happens in the head is different from an abscess which happens in the belly: and an abscess which happens in a fleshy part is different from an abscess which happens in a joint: and each one of them has its own judgment concerning the operation.
And concerning abscesses there are some which do not require perforation except after the completion of health in them, and the completion of their nourishment: and there are some which require perforation while they are raw and have not matured according to their nourishment.
For example, abscesses which are close to joints
Because an abscess when it happens near a joint and prolongs itself, until it putrefies, which is around it, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: which is the cause of the closer contraction of that limb
Or an abscess near a principal limb: because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will cause pain or swelling or both in that principal limb
If it be close to a hair, perforate it therefore immediately: and it is not necessary, when perforating, to leave a crude stool, not fully digested, except when it is close to a hair, that it not putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the rectum, and make a fistula, or remain in its termination which does not heal.
It is necessary to know the time of perforation of the stool, which is mature according to its completion: and it is with respect to the sedation of pain, recession of fever, and decrease of redness, and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the stool: and the rest of the signs.
It is necessary to make the perforation in a lower place of the stool, and immediately above the prominence: and let the perforation be wide in length of the body, if the stool is in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of laceration, and strings, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all the places equally, where there is no reflection
And in those which reflect, act with the perforation according to that member.
When the stool is in places of hardness: then it is better to omit the perforation of it, until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to its completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, you prolong the course of the disease from that, and there is much pain and swelling, and perhaps they will swell its lips, and depth of it
And some stools are perforated according to the width of the body in necessity: or according to that which the member requires
And it is necessary to administer in small stools a moderate perforation, and in large stools a wide perforation: or many sections similar to the magnitude of the stool.
And there are from the apostemata, in which it is necessary that the skin should be concaved: and it occurs when it has been made like a cloth, and has been made at the end, because it is dead: as it happens with many things, as with a dubelet and with boils.
And there are those which are cut through with a section having three angles. And there are those which occur like the figure of a myrtle leaf
Like a groin apostema
And there are those in which a round section and a lunar section are administered: and similar things are from sections
That which has no head, like the superficial lenious apostemata, must be perforated through a superficial perforation or flattened only.
And it is necessary, when the apostema is great and has collected much matter, to perforate it, so that it does not burst and draw out all the pus at once: rather extract from it a part of it immediately Then anoint the apostema until another day. Then extract some of the pus again.
Do this many times according to the degree [of severity]: especially if the patient is weak of spirit, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old man: for the spirit of the animal is dissolved from the animal with the evacuation of the matter: and perhaps the patient will die, and you will not perceive it.
Therefore take heed of this chapter, for it contains great caution
And after you have perforated the apostema, which you have perforated, it is necessary to remove the wool: and consider carefully If there is a small wound from a fracture, either perform a plane section or administer lead from an old vessel. If the fracture is large and there are many holes in the perforation, it is necessary to administer medication into every wound until it reaches the innermost area
If you have excised a piece of skin from the fracture, or if you have bound the circumference too tightly: then you must apply an old vessel or as much lint as before, or cover it with wool without moisture: and keep it tight until the third day.
Then remove it and care for it as is necessary until it heals
If there is bleeding during your operation in this hour, then administer cold water and vinegar, after they have been absorbed into a cloth: and put it over the place of bleeding profusely
If the bleeding persists, then you must administer a regimen and treatment, and powders, which I have mentioned in many places in this book, and from experience.
If it is winter and the place of the fracture is full of nerves: then you must infuse lead with wine, and warm fat: and put it over the place.
And if it is summer and the place is fleshy: then you must put soaked sponges in water, or fat, or both with wine and fat, and keep it cold. Until then, as we have said, on the third day you must drain the fracture, remove it, and administer treatment that is suitable for it until it heals.
This is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of fractures in general
According to the method of distinguishing the signs, as I have already mentioned concerning every apostemate, how it is possible to cure it is explained in a singular chapter.
Chapter XLI.
Regarding the excision of apostemata that occur in the scalp.
Small apostemata appear on the scalp and are of the kind known as knots, which contain tunica, which is like a sack: they are like little roosters' houses
There are many kinds of these.
Some are hard: others contain moisture like mud: others moisture like wet clay and stones.
In the excision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as one does not damage the arteries during the process.
The operation in the excision of these is as one must learn with the instrument which has a form like that which comes after these, which is called Intromissum, until you know what it contains
For if it contains moisture, dry it out by cutting through it superficially: in this way.
Begin from line b to line g. When the moisture has been evacuated, remove the knot which contains it, do not leave any residue behind
There is much risk if any residue is left behind
Then immerse the area in Egyptian unguent if it is present: or in water and salt: and apply with the willow and let it go until tomorrow This dries up what remains of the knot
Then repeat above the said hip with the second and third finger, and if you find it not yielding, until you are confident that nothing remains of the boil, then indeed care for the wound with an unguent until it is healed.
If there is a nodulus of fat remaining in the abscess: then dry that up again in this fashion, with a section having the shape of a cross: and project the nails between the two lobes of the section, and scour it from every part: and try your skill in extracting the sac which contains it.</start_of_turn>
If, however, it makes resistance against you, an artery is probably involved, then do as I shall tell you afterwards.</start_of_turn>
Do the same with a stone abscess, by section and care in the same way as before.</start_of_turn
And the section of a stone abscess is easier: because there is little blood and moisture.</start_of_turn And sometimes there are hours of abscesses which are in the head of some men, in which there is no moisture at all.</start_of_turn
Which is: because I have seen above an abscess in the head of a maidservant, and I found the abscess like a solid stone, hard, rough, white, which could not be broken: and if it was pierced with anything, it would burst.</start_of_turn
In other abscesses of the head which occur, besides these, such as abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the section of them is all superficial.</start_of_turn

Then care for that which is fitting for them from the care of the throat section
Chapter 42.
Many of these wounds happen in the throat, under the armpits, and in the groin: and sometimes in other parts of the body.
And over these which happen in the throat, there are one or many: and they are generated from each one: and every Strophe is within its own tunic, as in the nodes and the head wounds which we have mentioned
There are many kinds of these Strophes And from these there are soft: and there are those which contain humours: and there are malignant ones, which do not obey care
In this way, as the good humours are disposed in the touch, whose mark is the proximity of the colour of the skin, and which moves to all parts, and is not connected with the nerves of the throat, neither with the gullet, neither with the arteries, neither is it deep: it should be cut off with a superficial section from the upper part to the lower part of the body from the line, b. to the line, g. and be stripped from all sides: and extend two lips of the skin with a single layer, or with several layers if necessary, as we said in the head wounds, and pull out the contents little by little.
And it is with caution that one does not touch the bone, or the nerve
And let the blade be neither too acute nor too blunt: so as not to add your hand to the incision, or to frighten the patient, and cut only what is necessary
If, however, you cut a vein, or an artery, or are hindered by the operation, then put in the wound a piece of charred wood, or some powders which absorb blood, and tighten the wound, and leave it until the pain of the wound has ceased, and the wound has become soft: or else you will begin to putrefy it
Blood is severed by the clamp Redi therefore to the operation, until completion.
Then inquire with your finger, if anything remains from the other trocars: therefore cut easily, and wash away.
If it is in the root of the trocar a large one: then it is necessary that you do not cut that trocar from its root: rather it is necessary that you bind it with a strong doubled thread, and let it go until it falls off by itself without any pressure
Then apply a sponge soaked in Egyptian unguent under the area that you have submerged in the blood Then care for yourself.
If the trocar is cut entirely, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound: and hold these themselves immediately, after you have washed because there remains no excess.
If however you find that there remains excess blood because of the size of the trocar, then it is necessary that you extend to the cutting of its root and extract it: and administer the suture as we have said.
And what is contained from the trocar must be perforated with a superficial perforation: so that its maturity may appear: and place the perforation from that which follows below the body, as we have said
Then administer after the perforation, sponges with unguents, and similar things, so that it may consume that which remains from the corruption, until when you wash the wound will be found to be healed: then care for yourself with the unguents of generation of flesh until it is healed
Chapter XLIII.
Regarding the excision of the epiglottis from the epiglottic cartilage accidentally in the gut.
They say that the ancients performed this excision of the epiglottis, and no one in our land has ever done it: and this is the record of their sermon
When the throat is swollen with swelling of the epiglottis, it is necessary to make an incision of the epiglottis: for if this does not take place, all the veins and lungs will be weakened.
When there is a hard swelling in the throat, in the gullet, or in the tonsils, when there is no inflammation of the channel, it is necessary to perform an incision of the epiglottis to escape from the destruction which results from the obstruction.
It is necessary therefore, when we wish to perform this operation, to cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision in the middle of the two circles, so that the section is in the fat, not in the cartilage: and this is the most suitable place for the incision, because it is devoid of flesh, and the blood vessels from it are long.
If the physician is timid, it is necessary to extend the skin of the throat with one finger. Then find the skin, until it reaches the channel: there will be bleeding from it at that point
Then cut the fat which we have mentioned: and it is signified over the incision of the channel by the phlegm which comes out of it, when it is ruptured, and by the cutting of the vessel.
And let the wound be open for some time.
When the time has passed in which the obstruction has disappeared, gather together the two lips of the wound in the skin, and close it only with skin without cartilage.
Then give medicines which cause the flesh to heal, until it is healed
Thus spoke Albucasis. The sum of this sermon which we have narrated is not an exposition, except when someone has seen that some of the aftereffects of the disease have already become softened in the gut of the patient: and the patient has died, and he desires to cut across his desire. They go to dissect Epiglottis so that the patient can breathe in the place of the wound which is causing his respiration to be difficult, and they rejoice at his death. Because of this they have commanded the opening of the wound until the malice of the disease has passed away. And it is the malice of this disease for three days, and like itself. Then they have commanded the closing of the wound and its treatment until it is healed.
And what I have seen myself is that a handmaid took a knife and put it into his gut, and it went into the part of the lung canal.
I was called to his care, and I found him spitting blood, like a dead man.
I saw that there was a wound there, and blood had escaped from the small wound I was amazed, because neither the wound had burst open, nor did the intestines guide her: and the wind was coming out of the wound.
I did this, and I closed the wound, and I treated him, until he was healed: and the handmaid did not suffer anything, except that she coughed in the water. And she returned after a few days to a better state of health.
Henceforth it is clear that there is no fear in the section of Epiglottis
About the section of the hernia of the gut.
Chapter XLIV.
This aftereffect is called a hernia of the gut, is a great aftereffect, according to the colour of the body: and it is very common in women
There are two possibilities: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
Certainly not natural is the origin of it
Accidental is indeed according to two modes: one of which is like a knot of fat. The other mode is like the outgrowth of an artery from a knotting And in this is fear, and it is not right to assume it simply with the iron, except in the case where it is slight: you must try it and investigate it with the intestines If indeed you find it like a knot of fat, and it is not suspicious with any other swelling: then dry it off like knots are dried: and extract it with the thing which contains it from the cyst, if it is in a cyst: or if not, then explore all of it Then take care of the place with the thing which should be cured until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.</start_of_turn>
Regarding the cutting of things which are greater than knots.
There are many kinds of knots, and I have mentioned all of their kinds in the explanation: and it is necessary to know the difference between a knot and an extrusion in case of doubt
I say, when an extrusion has become lukewarm, and fever, and pain which accompany it, until the heat of excess has passed away and the putrefaction has ceased: then indeed calm down the fever and heat
When a knot does not have lukewarmness, neither fever, neither pain, and contains within itself a cyst which is its own sac, which is according to the colour of the body: and it is an inclusion like millet, and makes like batter, and is greater and smaller.
There are two kinds: either fat or containing moisture. The colours of moisture are many, as I said in the discourse on moisture
It is necessary therefore, when you are dealing with the cure of a Node, to experiment with it and inquire about it first with the instrument which is called the Intromissum, as its form will be found in the following chapter
The way to inquire about the posterior parts and of all Nodes is to take hold of this instrument and introduce it into the moist place which you find in the posterior part: and withdraw your fingers gradually until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin Then finally touch the hand according to the measure of the size of the posterior part
Then withdraw the Intromissum and consider what has come out through its wound
If therefore moisture is running out, whatever its colour: then dry it out with a superficial section, as I have said in the remaining sections concerning the posterior part
If it does not run out, then you know that it is fat: dry it out therefore with a section having the shape of a cross, as it has been taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and scrape the skin from all sides with ease: and hold the fat in place if you can, until you have extracted it whole with the Node
If however the sac is ruptured during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it whole: much the same happens in this case, then extract it fragment by fragment, until nothing remains of it
For if anything remains of it, little or much, the Node returns in most cases.
If it swells and remains small: then make a hole beside your completion and pour in corrosive powders of sharpness: and bind up the hole, and place above it what rests upon the soft cushion, and care for it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
If there is a large knot, then cut through its two lips, and care for it as that which has been incarnated
If however there is a swelling that is painful, or that does not swell: then cleanse it, and make an incision in the place where the swelling is greatest, and drain it out for two or three days, until the swelling has gone down, and the blood has clotted: and then return to the original condition
Chapter XLVI.
As to the instruments for the cutting and perforating of the wound.
There are three forms of instruments for the introduction of instruments, and there are three sorts.
The first is large, the second is medium, and the third is small

And these are the forms of the instruments of the experienced, and no, that is, perforating: and one of them is called the beris, that is, the perforator, and there are three sorts
The first is large, the second is medium, and the third is small
They join together, that they may investigate the swelling, and the exits, and the fistula, and the abscesses from that which is between the bones
And others are round, and square, and flat, like a great needle which sews under silk, from citrine or from air, or from iron, or from silver
And the better things are those which come from the citron.
And experience is also derived from black lead, which, when applied to these fistulas, causes them to reflect like glass with their smoothness.
There are three things in number. For as regards the size of the wound, it is either wide, medium, or narrow, according to the quantity of the diameter of the fistula and the degree of its constriction
The shape of the wounds is diverse, and there are many kinds of them
For as regards their kind, there are simple wounds, which have but one curvature: and there are three kinds of them, like liquids, large, medium, and small

The exhalations of the veins when the flow of blood is broken: and there are three kinds, large, medium, and small
They are formed from air, or from lemon juice, round, about the length of a palm, like water: and they are transparent.
And it is necessary that these kinds of veins be large and small with you: in order to break the blood quickly when necessary, and then when it is not there a doctor is not present.
However, do not use these things for the breaking of blood in any place of the body, and do not use them except in places where there is carnation: such as wounds of the leg, and hip, and wounds of the arm, and breast, and belly, and groin, and similar places, from wounds of carnation, humors.

Concerning the swelling which occurs from the torsion of nerves.
Concerning the swelling which occurs from the torsion of nerves.
Just as swelling occurs in arteries and veins due to torsion, in the same way swelling occurs in nerves: which occurs either from percussion or from excessive tension, or in like manner: and it often happens in the armpits, or the calves, and in every place where nerves are twisted together: and it is a serious swelling.
It is similar in color to the rest of the body, and it is found in various positions without pain: and when it is grasped with force, a numbness similar to that of intoxication is felt in it
It is not a swelling that is collected in the depths of the body, but rather under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forward or backward or downwards.
Because it is in the twisted parts, it is not necessary to suture it with iron.</start_of_turn> Perhaps some contraction will occur against it.

Of the care of the belly wound.

Or if it is because of moisture, it is soft: then it does not hide when you touch it with your hand, neither does it tighten, neither does it increase in size.
Or if it is because of blood, then it appears with these signs like a black clot at the edge of the wound

On the treatment of children, and the care for an error in an accident.
Circumcision is not the completion of continuity, like other wounds: indeed, when it is done by our operation, by our will, and when its benefit is in children themselves, it is necessary that we sign it to indicate that we have performed a good operation, and that we have not thereby diminished the benefit.
I say therefore that the ancients did not speak of circumcision in any of their books: because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only by experience that we have learned it
Because I have found a greater part of the artifices, and phlebotomies, useful for purification by circumcision, with a knife, and with nails: and that they are used for the insane, and bound with thread, and incised with a nail.
And I myself have tried all these methods, and I have not found a better purification than by knife and thread
For in purification with a knife, much skin is often thrown away. For the skin of the prepuce has two tunics: so perhaps the upper skin is cut, and the lower tunic is threatened: it is therefore necessary to make an incision to cut the other, and to avoid future pain And when purification is done by the insane, that is, by digitalis, as the physicians do, the incision of the extremity of the penis is not secured: for perhaps it enters through its foramen
In purification with a nail, perhaps the iron will go through, and will corrupt your operation: and the skin of a child is short in nature, and many are born thus, which does not befit purification: and I have seen this
Purification with a knife and thread is a good experiment that I have undertaken: for the knives are proportional to the incision: because the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below When you have finished with your hand, two acute angles are formed, and they meet according to the operation of one in the same time: and it makes a compression similar in figure, containing the skin of the limb from all sides, so that no error occurs at all.
The proper way to perform the operation is to estimate the extent of the wound, especially if there is someone who understands the matter, because you must not do anything unless you tie the thread in the limb itself: and you let it go until tomorrow.
Then make him laugh and be merry in every way that you can, from which he will recover his intelligence. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and do not sit: and hide the instruments in your sleeve or under your foot, so that no boy sees them, neither anything of the instruments
Then insert your hand into the limb and blow into the skin, and draw it up, until the head of the limb comes out. Then loosen what is gathered in that cavity.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Then tie the lower part with a little of the ligament alongside Then take hold with your index finger and thumb, the place of the lower ligament with a good hold, and cut between the two ligaments. Then pull the skin up quickly and extract the head of the limb. Then let the blood run slowly (for it is better thus) and put powdered sulfur on: and put a bandage over the place where the swelling has gone down, crushed with recent good salt: and let it go over it until tomorrow: and take care of the rest of the treatment until he is healed
The shape of the knife for cutting is thus.
Two blades extend at the extremities, and their convergence: do not extend into the substance of the nail: and let the length of the two be equal to the length of two thumbs together
The error which occurs in cutting is: perhaps the entire intrinsic skin or a part of it escapes at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend your hand with your nail before the cutting of the place: and you cut according to the equality.
If you are unable to hold the hand with your nail, then extract the hand with your thumb and cut the place.
If, however, three days have passed and what is under the nail remains swollen, remove the nail: then leave it until the swelling subsides, and when it has become soft, remove it easily and cut it as has been prepared before: and take care that you avoid the head of the nail It will not cause him any harm.

Therefore care for it as we have said until it heals.

On the care of the intestines.

(The text continues with a section on castration.)
You know that castration is forbidden in our law: and for that reason I will not mention it in this book: and it is not necessary to mention it, except for two cases.
Of which one is that it is in the science of medicine when it is inquired about: and to know its treatment when it happens.
And the other case is that we often need castration for some animals, because of our desires concerning them, such as asses, hinds, and cats, and the like of animals
I say therefore that castration is done in two ways. Or by cutting off. Or by incision, and cutting through
In which way, however, which is done by attrition, the operation is this: that we make the animal sit in warm water, until the testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down: and then cut off the testicles with your hand, until they are resolved and do not appear to the touch
In castratione autem per fissuram et sectionem, oportet ut teneas animal, et premas cutem testiculorum eius cum manu sinistra.
In castration, however, through incision and section, it is necessary to keep the animal still, and to clamp the skin of the testicles with the left hand.
And this method of castration is better than that by attrition: for the attrition may perhaps leave something behind from the testicles, which the animal desires to have coitus.
Then care for the wound, until it has healed.

Chapter LX.
The obstetrical doctrine, concerning how to care for the embryos of women, according to an unnatural form of birth.
It is necessary for the obstetrician to know in the first place the figure of natural birth.
From the signs therefore it is known that when a woman is pressed by the waters below and wishes to smell the air, and alleviates above what is above her from the birth, and hastens the issue of the embryo: then you know that this birth is natural, and is the issue above its head, and the second with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel
When therefore these signs are present, then it is necessary to compress the womb so that the embryo comes down quickly
For when it descends above its head, it descends with the second part and is delivered from these superabundances by a good delivery
Whoever is beyond this birth is unnatural, harmful
For when the fetus comes out above its feet, and the body of it goes before the head, and the feet and hands together, or one foot: or the head and the hand: or the belly: and perhaps turns upon its collar, and similar things from the harmful figures.
It is necessary for the Obstetrician to be prudent, cautious in all these matters, and to beware of error and decomposition: for I shall show every figure and how the temperament is in each one, and it is signified by that, and it stands above itself.
When the fetus has gone beyond its head during its natural egress, and the head becomes engaged above the woman, and delivery becomes difficult, and the power of its courage is being crippled: then let the woman sit down, or let the women hold her up: and let the vapor of her breath be driven away with fumigation of rue and with oily substances.
Then the Obstetrician inserts his fingers between the toes of the patient, and finds the second one, and seizes it with his nail, until he has drawn out what is in it from the moisture: and he presses down the belly of the woman until the fetus comes down.
If it does not come down, then it is necessary to instill the woman with a cathartic of rue with oil.
Then command her to drink after the catharsis, that she may compress: and make her lie down with her chin resting on her chest for an hour. The fetus will come out immediately.
If two hands of the fetus come out at once, it is necessary to reduce them gradually with ease If it does not obey in the birth, then let the woman sit down and elevate her feet above the ground: then press down on her seat towards the earth: and the woman shall be held, so that she does not fall from the concussion
If, however, the hands do not come out, and the fetus dies: then cut down the sides and extract the fetus: and bind up the hand with cloth, and extract it with evenness: for it will come out itself
The embryo's exit is above its feet. When the fetus exits above its feet, it is necessary to drive it upwards: then gradually bend it: afterwards it will be even.
Then take hold of one foot and gradually extend it
When they emerge up to the hips, then command it to bend down, so that it will be crushed, and make it exhale with force: it will come out, God willing
And if it does not come out as we have said, then gradually reduce the fetus until you have set it according to its natural shape. For it will come out by itself when its time comes.
If it is delayed as regards all that we have said: then take from the thick mucus above, and salt, and gum dissolved: and pierce all through in the belly with a good blow: then draw out with the woman's palm and the lower part of her belly Then make it sit in warm water, until the afterbirth follows naturally

Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.</start_of_turn
The fistulas which arise in the lower part are Nodatio and Grossitudo which happen close to the bones of the limb: either in the space of one part or in several.
When the Nodatio is old, it opens, and white watery moisture or fine pus runs from it
And sometimes these Fistulas are penetrating Therefore the penetrating ones are recognized by the fact that they issue from discharge and swelling, when the patient uses the egestion: and perhaps they issue from worms.
And these are the kinds of Fistulas when they are penetrating until they reach the bone and the marrow of the limb And they are penetrating until they reach the hip joint and the tail bone node
From this it is known that a penetrating Fistula until it reaches the limb is also not penetrating, as when you put your finger into the limb: and you put in a needle fine in the Fistula from the air or from the iron, when there is no swelling.
When there is swelling in this, then put in the needle fine from lead or horsehair until you feel the needle, or put in the hair in your finger
If you do not feel the needle itself, neither does it go out from the discharge, nor swelling, nor worms as I said: then you know that it is not penetrating: so proceed to the treatment then
And when the Fistula is penetrating until it reaches the bone or the bladder: then the significance of it is the discharge of urine from it, and the prohibition of this until it is consolidated with the physicians.
If the wound extends up to the hip joint or the hip bone: then its sign is that the limb will hang down, if there is no twist in it up to the bone: and there will be pain in the surrounding parts, and pus will always come from it; and no remedy will be of any avail, neither will it be consolidated with ointment.
In all such penetrating wounds there is no cure: the cure for them is toil and perseverance, from the stupid doctors on whom they are formed.</start_of_turn>
But in those which are neither penetrating nor old, healing is hoped for with rest: as I have already said, and as I have shown: that the sick man should lie between your hands over his back, and elevate his limbs up to above his hips, and that his thighs should be inclined towards his belly.</start_of_turn> Then introduce lead or air, if there is a twist in the wound, until you know where the limb hangs.</start_of_turn>
If, however, the sick man feels about his limb: then it is necessary to insert your finger into the wound.</start_of_turn
If you feel in your finger that the limb is being pierced by itself, as well as between your finger and the flesh, or bone: then you know certainly that it is penetrating: there is no labour in this, for it is not its cure, as we have said.</start_of_turn>

Head wound.</start_of_turn
I have already spoken in the discussion of the treatment of wounds which agrees with the book, and I will now speak of the treatment of these with the hand and with the help of physicians according to the complement.</start_of_turn
I say therefore, that wounds are varied according to the cause of the wound and according to the place where the wound falls.</start_of_turn
The wounds which are caused by many things are like the blows of a stone: or the cutting of a sword or knife: or the percussion with a lance or stick or arrow: and the like.</start_of_turn
Wounds which are caused by the place of the body are like a wound which falls on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the belly, or an arm: and the like of these from all parts of the body.</start_of_turn
I am now going to narrate the treatment of certain wounds, so that I can set forth the ratiocination and rule concerning the rest of the wounds.
I shall begin with simple head wounds: for the memory of compound wounds will arise at the beginning of the third chapter
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs in the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: then consider, if it is from a stone or something similar, and if only the skin is split: then it is a large wound, and the time of healing is long for a wound of such severity: therefore do this: make an immediate cephalic bleed (phlebotomy): do not delay this; and extract from him from the blood according to the quantity of his virtue: if there is nothing to prevent it, especially if there is little blood flowing from the wound And let the phlebotomy be from the side opposite the wound
And let the phlebotomy be from the side opposite the wound And let the patient be careful from repletion, from wine, and from coarse food
If anything hinders the reduction (of the wound), then let him abstain from food, and not approach meat or drink wine
And apply upon the wound if it happens that there is a blood clot, a soft compress of saffron only, or with wine in which there is stypticity.
And if you are secure from the blood clot, then place over the wound, if there is still some blood in it, and it has not become black, the powder which I shall tell you of later: and if it has become black, then place over it some unguent, until it is healed
And if the wound is large, or is from a wound with a knife, or similar: and the edges do not adhere by themselves with little stitches, then unite them with sutures, according to what I shall narrate concerning the sutures of the internal wounds
If, however, a vein has been detected by the bone, and hangs loose, and its retention is not possible, except in a small suspending apparatus: then cut it off. Care for the vein with the physicians, and restore it in the place of the skin, and command the patient to eat nourishing foods, such as heads and cooked wheat, until the pulse returns: and let them be similar.
If you find in the flesh putrefaction, and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut off the putrefied part throughout, and throw it away: then care for the wound
If, however, in his wounds there is an artery, or a vein from which blood flows: and it is not severed by the physicians, then inquire about the artery. If, therefore, you find it untied, cut it off with Spatumili, or ligate it.</start_of_turn> If necessity compels you when it does not conform to what we have said, then cauterize it, until the blood is severed.</start_of_turn>
If, however, these wounds are small and simple, then the matter is easy and light: thus as to make powder of them over this powder: because it consolidates them before they change them by air.</start_of_turn
The manner of making powder of the flesh.</start_of_turn> Olive oil one part, dragon's blood two parts, and unslaked lime three parts: mix them all together thoroughly, and grind them: and make from this a paste, and bind it with good constriction, until it adheres well to itself, and coagulates with the blood.</start_of_turn
Then bind it up with sponges, and send it away, and do not wash it until it remains, so that it does not putrefy.</start_of_turn Until it is healed, it is not eradicated by medicine from that.
