If the ulcer is deep, or it breaks into that sinus in its lower part, where pus is collected, then perforate it in a depressed place in that.
If an artery is cut in the ulcer, and its blood flow is consequent: then separate it, or dry it up, and ligate it: or cauterize it, if need be for that.</start_of_turn>

If there is a percussion of a lance or a knife, and you see its depth, then consider it.</start_of_turn If any blood proceeds from a wound when the patient desires to draw it out, then you know that the wound is mortal.
If it is not deep and is accompanied by its own blood: then you do not cauterize it in the first hour, neither do you ligate it, lest the blood be retained in its depth, and thereby cause the patient's death: but apply in it a strong dressing, and evacuate it in two days.
If indeed no dressing is present in the wound: then apply in the mouth of the wound a thick wool cushion, that it may absorb what proceeds from it from the humilities: and cause the patient to sleep over the wound, that the blood which collects in it may be cured
If indeed more than three days or more have passed from the wound and the patient has neither spasm nor bad tremor of the cord, neither bad congestion in the desire to urinate, and maintains all the other conditions: then you know that the wound is healed. Treat it therefore with care and with medicines, and with the rest of the cure, until it is healed.
If indeed the healing is excused, and it always opens: then you know that a fistula has been made. Treat it therefore from its head to its tail
And if the wound is from a cut of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then treat it as if it were large: or with powder if it is small
But if it has made an imprint in the bone, and has burst from it: then inquire about the wound, and proceed to extract the fragment, if they are separated. But if they are not separated: then leave them alone, until the wound putrefies, so that the extraction of them may be easier.
The judgment concerning the remaining wounds on the other limbs is that of the physician.
Concerning the wounds of the belly, the intestines, and their exits
The disruption that occurs within the belly is great, medium, or small.
From the great comes the evacuation of the intestines, or a number of intestines
Therefore the introduction of the bowels, and their suture, is more difficult and demanding
And the small disruption is also difficult in another way Therefore, it follows that the reduction of these is more difficult if you do not immediately perform the introduction into its place: for it swells, opens, and becomes thickened: which makes its entrance difficult.
For this reason, the medium disruption is better: because with it the reduction of the intestines is not difficult, as it is with the small or great disruptions
You know that the suture of the bowels is done in four ways.
Two sutures are common in the suture of the belly, and in the suture of the rest of the limbs: and two other sutures are done properly with the rest of the wounds However, there are other proper methods when suturing the belly: and I shall narrate them all with their exposition and their declaration.
I say, because when a wound is small, and something from the intestine comes out of it, and it is difficult to reduce it: then this is due to one of the two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we have said: or because there is inflation in the intestine due to the cold of the air.
When it is thus, then it is necessary to heat it, so that you may immerse the sponge or wet bag in tepid water alone. Or boil it in water with barley, pepper, and spicery, and embrace the intestines with these, until the swelling subsides.
When it has made the liquid in which there is stickiness: it is better than plain water in resolving the swelling
When the swelling has been resolved, then moisten the intestines with the water in which it has been boiled, or with vinegar. For in this way it will soothe its entrance with a small thing.
If however it escapes after this treatment, then you will find it in slight disruption, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut off
The first treatment is better when it is prepared, than when it is forced, and does not cause a pressing of the cutting instrument beyond what is necessary and the defect of the instrument itself
Let one part be acute and the other not acute, and the outer part should not be like that of Spatumilis, but rather be somewhat blunt: and this is like a chick's foot, as regards the liquid
When the vessel has been strengthened and the intestine has been introduced, then it is necessary to reduce it according to its natural form in its own place, if possible over that: for this is better
When the rupture is wide at the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary to make the sick man lie on his back, and place his knees higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his feet
If there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then set your intention and consideration on this, that the part which has the wound be above the other part.
And thus it is necessary, that you should look at these things in great wounds and in medium wounds
In small wounds, set it according to what is prepared for you
Then make it present between your hands, your pious assistant who will hold the whole disruption of the hand: and gather its lips. Then take from it whatever follows the suture something after something: and this is the method of common suture of one
And it is, that you take the needle, or the number of needles according to the quantity of the wound Then send from the extremity of the disruption the quantity of the little finger, that is, the physician: and fix the needle once having passed it into the flesh in two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of fat which is under the skin, until you make it penetrate from another part, and you have gathered together the two extremities of the skin, and the two extremities of fat: and there are four orders.
Then string with a double thread around the circumference of the needle several times from two parts together, until two lips of the wound are gathered together with a decent aggregation.
Then send away the length of the little finger also, and fix in the flesh another needle: afterwards draw back the seam with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Do not cease doing this until you have completed the constriction of the whole wound
And the needles are medium between the length and the thickness: because the fine needles slip easily through the flesh: and the thick ones are difficult to enter the skin: because of this it is necessary that they be medium in thickness, and long.<start-of-turn>
Neither should the needle be left protruding at two distant points of the skin near each other: for thereby the flesh will be more likely to split or tear before consolidation has taken place, and will even remain elongated even after stitching, thereby hindering consolidation.
Neither should the needle be left protruding at two distant points of the skin near each other: for thereby the flesh will be more likely to split or tear before consolidation has taken place, and will even remain elongated even after stitching, thereby hindering consolidation.
And thus is the kind of suture which is more suitable for small wounds. For in stitching, one or two needles, or somewhat more, will suffice.
The second common kind of suture is that in which the four extremities are held together by two stitches: two of the skin and two of the fat with the needle in which the thread is wound being of equal thickness and size
And when you have penetrated with the needle through the four extremities, reduce the needle from the part from which you began, so that the wound may be held by the remaining thread: as is the case with the stitching of cloth by which cloth is bound, and put between every suture as much of the thickness of a little finger
This kind of suture is well known to men
And when I performed this suture on a wound which had been caused by a knife in its belly, which was ruptured through and through from the palm of the hand, and had burst out from the intestines almost two palms from the middle of the intestine, and there was a rupture in the middle of the belly
I reduced the wound accordingly after I had set the intestines outside the wound for twenty-four hours
The limb has been consolidated for about fifteen days and has healed: I have treated it and it is now cured. It has remained sound ever since, and has returned to its normal condition.
The physicians declared that it would not recover.</start_of_turn>
And miraculously, I cured it without medication.</start_of_turn> Since I was in a place where unguent was not to be found, and there was no doctor there.</start_of_turn> I put on the limb a warm compress twice a day, and I bathed it diligently with sweet water until it healed.</start_of_turn>
I used two kinds of stitches as described in the sermon.</start_of_turn>
And it is necessary to start with a fine suture: and you insert the point from the outside to the inside of the part.</start_of_turn
When the needle has penetrated the skin, and has passed through the tough part of the wound according to the length and breadth of the entire body: withdraw the point which you inserted at the beginning in the side where you introduced the needle, and make the needle pass through from the inside to the outside of the opposite side at the other end.</start_of_turn
When you have made the needle pass through, then make it pass again in the same place from the outside to the inside: and withdraw the point which is in this side, and make the needle pass through the opposite side from the inside to the outside: and make the needle pass through with the same direction as you made it penetrate in the point which is in the part, until it penetrates the whole thing.</start_of_turn
And then start from this side also, and join it with the opposite side, and withdraw the needle from the skin where it penetrated.</start_of_turn
Then reduce the skin to that same area, and scrape off the outer extremity which is on the other side, along with the inner one: and extract from the skin that which is from its part, and make it one with the other, and the other with the first: and make the latter one like the former, until the outer surface is complete according to that example.
The sum of all these sutures is that of the skin-stitches which are to be made for the skin: so that the scraped area may be made one with the remainder of the skin by means of the sutures which are to be made from the outer part of the extremity to the inner part, and from the inner part to the outer part, until the surface is complete.
The second kind of proper suture which Gale has mentioned.
This is how it is said that they should be made like the things which some physicians do, in order that every part may be gathered according to its kind: and that you should scrape off one extremity against the other, and the inner against the outer. And this is done according to what I have told you.
The liver should be placed against the inner side of the outer extremity, and the skin should be pierced through from the outside inwards in that place: and extract the two extremities. Then draw out the liver, and make the skin penetrate from the outside inwards through two extremities of the scraped area, namely both of them.
Then draw out both of them, and also make them penetrate from the inside outwards through the two outer extremities which are on the opposite side
This is the best way of stitching, and is the suture which requires the greatest memory
This is indeed what Galen has said.
And some have said. The suture within is corrected with two squares. When the suture is within, two squares are added. That is, you pass through the tissue with one thread: and begin with the needle that is towards you, and make it penetrate until it reaches the other side, following the direction of the suture which joins the tissues equally
And you know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly: then the suture of that is more difficult than the rest of the belly
The judgment of the ulcer of the wound is the judgment of the rest of the wounds.
When you follow the wound along with its blood, before it changes itself: and you reduce the intestine, and bring it close, and fix it: then you put on powdered ochre over the wound
When the air has already changed the wound: then you put on over it some unguents which are present, until they make pus: and the threads fall out, and they are consolidated, and it is healed And then care for it as for the healing of the rest of the wounds, until it is healed
But if there are no remedies at hand, then you put on over it immediately, from which point it begins to discharge pus, slightly fat: and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed
For you do not apply another remedy in many situations when the wounds are simple
If however you fear that the wound communicates with the principal parts of the body in the lesion: then it is necessary that you immerse fine wool yarn in lukewarm water, or in rose water: and put it around the parts which are between the groin and the armpits
If he feels pain, or swelling, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens to him), then he must anoint himself with vinegar of wine, black and warm: especially if the putrefaction has already penetrated the intestine, and an opening has been made through to its cavity.
And know this, that which is from the great intestine is easier to heal: and that which is from the small intestine is more difficult to heal.
But the intestine which is called the jejunum, does not endure healing from the wound that has happened to it throughout: and this is because of the multitude of its veins, and the greatness of their number, and because of the subtility of its body, and for its nearness to its own nature of nerves.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is pus, and you follow it, then reduce it according to your reduction which you make of the intestines equally.
If, however, it has passed beyond that space, and has become black or dark: then it is necessary to string it with thread above the place which has become defiled, in order that the blood does not flow out from it. For in the pus there are veins and arteries.
Then cut through that which is beyond that ligament, and place two hanging threads from the lower part of the wound outside of it: in order to support over that which attracts it, and to extract it from the case of the pus, and to emit the pus of the wound.
Remember the wound that happened in the Intestine
When disruption happens in the Intestine, and it is small: then it is possible, in some people, that it may be healed: because I saw that which was wounded in the middle of the stomach by the blow of a lance, and the wound was ancient: and a fistula was made, and it went out from it egestion, and swelling.
I remain in attendance until he is cured, for I am not relieved until his health has recovered completely: neither do I cease until he is fully healed and his position is firm.
Once I have settled in his place, I fear over the patient, lest something bad should happen to him: and nothing bad has happened to him from that which might have happened to him. I remain in better positions, eating and drinking, trusting and having hope, and I bathed him and exercised him in his diet.
And some of the experimenters say, when there is a wound in the intestines and it is slight: then it must be bound in this way. And it is like taking large ants. Then join together two lips of the wound, and put an ant on one of those which have an open jaw above two lips of the wound When the head is over them, and it binds its jaw, cuts off its head, and it will adhere to it, and will not break
Then put another ant close to the first, and do not cease doing this until the wound is complete
Then reduce it, and its wound. These heads remain annexed to the intestines until the intestines are fed and healed, and do not cause pain to the patient
And it is possible to bind the intestines even with a fine thread: which has been taken from the intestines of an animal, and is annexed to it when it is introduced into the belly
And take the extremity of this thread from the intestines, and remove a great deal of it. Then the ligament at its extremity is wrapped with a fine thread. Then introduce the thread into the knot, and pass it through with the gut Then tighten the knot.
And thus is the manner of stitching with needles and gut, except for some cases of great confidence and hope.
You know that a large, wide vessel, especially if it is within a graceful gut: in such a case there is no skill except meticulous suture, and no healing is possible
With regard to care of wounds and fistula.
You know that every wound, or fistula when it has become old and hardened, and has become a ulcer, and does not consolidate, and emits pus constantly, and does not heal: in such a case it is called universally in every part of the body a fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.</start_of_turn>
And a fistula is indeed according to truth a filtered node, hard, white: which does not cause pain, which has the consistency of a feather of a bird: and for this reason they call some of them Risati, that is, feathers.</start_of_turn
And sometimes in certain hours it is wet, which emits pus.</start_of_turn And sometimes the wetness may split in certain hours.</start_of_turn And sometimes there is much of this wetness, and little: and it is large, and is soft.</start_of_turn
And as I have said previously in the discourse, when I said that every ulcer which does not heal, and which is not born in this flesh, is not because of one cause.</start_of_turn
When one is in a state of scarcity of blood in the body: or because of the malice of the blood itself: or because of the hardness of the flesh between the labia, which hinders the birth of flesh: or because of the abundance of pus and phlegm: or because the ulcer in itself is putrid, and the matter which discharges from it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment: or because corruption is falling in the region, and is a kind of pestilence: or by reason of property in the region, as for example in Caesaraugusta, in which there is great difficulty of digestion for ailment, and the healing of wounds is delayed by nature: or because in it is one bone, or a number of bones.
I have also mentioned all these causes and signs, and the treatments for them with physicians. It is necessary that you write here the treatment for fistula and swelling, which is done by one operation with the hand.
You know therefore that fistula can occur in all the members of the body. There are fistulas which extend to large veins and arteries, or nerves, or fat: or to the intestine, or the bladder, or to some of the bones: or to some of the vertebrae or to some of the compound joints: such as the hand, and the foot. And there are those which arise from proximity to the main limb and its parts of the same kind
For this reason, the fistulas are of this kind because of the difficulty of healing the ailment, and do not receive treatment, and are accompanied by labor and ignorance
If, however, any fistula does not reach this depth, and you hope and trust in the treatment of it, and wish to reach cognition of it, then attempt to drain it from the air, or from iron, if the fistula follows a straight course, and inquire about it with her/him.
If, however, there is a twist in the fistula, then you should investigate it with a fine lead weight. Since lead is flexible with its body, it will curve around the twist
If there are many openings in the fistula, it is not possible to perform an investigation with a weight on a hook.</start_of_turn> Explore the cavity from each opening through its own opening.</start_of_turn>
For moisture which you apply to explore will spread out over other openings and will run out of the cavity.</start_of_turn
Then inquire carefully in what way is it possible: whether it is a bone or a nerve, and whether the fistula is long or short: or whether it is a single fistula with many openings, and that lies throughout the whole, with the sum of your strength: with your questioning of the patient, and the finding of pain in compression with your hand over the place, and similar signs and symptoms accompanying the accident.</start_of_turn>
When you have stood in all these positions truly, then you can proceed to the treatment with confidence: and it is that you consider whether the fistula is clearly adjacent or in a remote place from the junction, or from a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of those places which I mentioned to you: then dry the fistula according to what has been said above, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupt tissues, which arise in its orifice from the flesh, or pus, and similar things: and treat it until it is healed.</start_of_turn
If, however, the fistula is of great depth and is according to the rule: then it is necessary to dissect it as deeply as possible.</start_of_turn Then expose the whole of its corrupt tissues.</start_of_turn
Then administer the ligature tightly in acute cases: and introduce it until you have grasped the fistula with the iron: do this repeatedly until the acute medicine has eaten away all that remains in the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with the sutures which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed
If, however, it does not heal by this, then cauterize it as we have said.
If, however, the cause of the fistula is bone, and you suspect this to be the case: then inquire about it, and dry it as I have said: if this does not suffice, or if there is any other limb, vein, or main artery which is opposed to this, as we have mentioned.
If, however, it is detected that there is bone in it, and there is some corruption and blackness: then burn it until the corruption of it is completely consumed Then care for it with that which nourishes it, until it is healed.
That if it does not heal, and pus comes out as before: then you know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption
Observe it carefully afterwards, and make reason for it, and treat it with the utmost care and skill
Then restore it If, however, it heals, that is good: and if not, you know that the corruption is in a profound depth of the body, which you were not able to overcome It is not in this way that you will overcome it, except by resorting to the natural order of things.
If the small bone is corrupted, and you are able to do so, draw it out with fine forceps which are suitable for this purpose.
If many bones are present, then take care to make an attraction of them all, and do not send anything away from the bones with your force.
If, however, there is any moisture in the incision, and there is no fear of error, then cut through the moisture and the flesh there, and free the bones.
If you are not prepared to extract the bones from their place at this hour, then put upon them something which will cause them to putrefy: and send them away, until they putrefy, that is, until the bones are freed from the flesh and moisture surrounding them
And keep the bandage so that it does not consolidate. And let the incision be firm as you made it in your operation And if you put upon it, if necessary, a bandage soaked in sulfur trioxide with oil: or a bandage soaked in Egyptian unguent, or any other unguents of your desire.
For when the moisture putrefies, and separates from the bones, it alleviates the loosening of the bones, and their attraction
If there is a large single bone, such as a thigh bone or a hip bone, and the like of them, which is corrupted, it is a great part: then take away that part last, until that black spot and corruption is removed from it. Then restore the bandage.
If, however, that which is corrupted is a great part, and the corruption has already proceeded to the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to cut through it and open it right up to the place where the corruption remains: then cure it, until it is consolidated
And I tell you this, which happened to me in my thigh: that you may take this as an example and aid in your operation.
A youth of about thirty years of age suffered pain in his leg, because something had become displaced from above downwards within the body, that is, from the interior, until it reached the leg, and there was great swelling: and there was no external cause of the matter.
The swelling had continued for a long time, with the error of the physicians, until the swelling was opened up, and many materials came out together with it And after this the healing was delayed, until the leg was bandaged, and many holes were made in it: from which pus and moisture of the body issued out
The matter was treated by many physicians for two years, and there was no skill in their art to be found in this matter, until I was called, and I applied a remedy to the leg, and the matters which were running out of the holes greatly decreased: even all the swelling of the body disappeared, and its colour became pale yellow.
There was an introduction of the hand into one of the holes, and the hand went up to the bone.
And then I examined all the holes, and I found that they were all connected with each other throughout the whole leg
I then treated it, and I applied a dressing over one of the holes, until I discovered a piece of bone: and I found that it was corrupted: for it was rotten, discoloured, and putrified, and perforated, until it had penetrated right up to the marrow
I saw that which had been detected was prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I concluded that there was no soundness in the bone except for that corruption which I had cut off and bound up And I extirpated it.
Then I order restoration of the wound with the consolidated physicians in a spacious area. It is not consolidated.
Then I attempted, and I found upon the second examination above the first: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I observed that there appeared to me also from that corruption
I attempted to restore it. It is not restored
I found therefore upon it again, and I did not cease to cut the bone piecemeal, and to try to restore it: and it is not restored, until I cut from the bone round about one palm, and I extracted that with the marrow
Then I restored it with the physicians, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and no further operation was necessary for this operation, and the section of the same, except for the disposition of the weakness of the patient, and his little tolerance, and my fear of death for him: because it was happening to him in every hour from the excessive evacuation of syncope.
He was healed completely: and was born in the place of the hard bone, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he convalesced in his own dispositions: and did not make him contradict in walking at all
But if the prominent bone in the body is fractured: then it is necessary to bind it up according to this method: that you take the ligament, and bind the bone at the extremity of the prominent bone: and command someone to extend it upwards: and put another ligament from a thick thread beside the first ligament.</start_of_turn> Then bind the ligament over the flesh, which is on the bone: and extend the extremity of it, so that you draw the flesh downwards: and you detect the flesh over the place where you wish to cut, so that it does not injure the flesh.</start_of_turn>
Place wood or a board under the lower bone in the center: for when you do this, it will not hinder that it will secrete the corrupted flesh.
The wound should be closed above the corrupted area without tension, so that there is no deformation of the bone's corruption: and it must not be visible in its appearance to the sense, and must incite to closure of its wound in another way.
If the bone is corrupted and is not prominent, continue the part of it with the part: and the corruption is in the middle of it or in the part itself: then remove the flesh from all parts of it whole Then place wood under it
Then secure the wound from the first part where the corruption is, until the closure is completed from the other side: and let the wound be as far from the corruption as possible, as we said before.
If the corruption is at the junction, cut through the corrupted junction, and secure the bone where the closure is completed on the other side.
If indeed the corruption is in the continuation of two junctions, then there is no wisdom in it except to burn it.
If the corruption is in the palm of the hand, or in the palm of the foot: then the matter is difficult to handle It is true that you must inquire about the nature of the corruption. Of the care of limbs, and the quality of blistering with the physicians.
When swelling occurs in some limb with pain, and the extent of the swelling is prolonged until collection of pus has taken place: then it is broken open, or pierced, and the pus which is in it is ejected, and the place which was filled with pus is left like a cavity: and the skin which is above it is like a thin veil which has been stripped off: and it does not reach its ultimate corruption, neither does it affect the bone, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it is called a sinus and not a fistula, although if its extent is prolonged, until corruption affects some parts of the limb, then it is called a fistula or swelling
And its care is that you cut through the whole skin as if you were rolling it: and especially when it is thin, and made like a veil: and trust that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.

Concerning the extraction of the vein of the leg

On the section above the navel.
The pain located above the navel is called Nakir in our land: it is a pain that occurs in some parts of the body, and then changes from one part to another.
And I myself have seen it happen, as I will tell you. I was called to a woman who was sick in some village And I saw her arm swollen, and I found an inflammation of the vein of the arm
And after an hour had passed, I saw another swelling appear with a worm, which went up towards his shoulder more quickly than is possible: just as silver flies from one place to another
The pain therefore departed from that place and settled in the shoulder
And after a while it went away throughout the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm
And she told me that it returned to its whole body, as I saw it
I am amazed at the speed of its change from one limb to another And I myself had never seen such a pain before like this in the same way as this But I have seen many people who have had pain changing from one limb to another: and I had never seen anything like that before in a woman.
I do not believe this is possible, except that it is because of the women of those regions who are known for their swelling veins: from there therefore appears to be a change of sensation throughout the body.
It is necessary that they do not appear according to this ratio in those who are delicate and have moist bodies, and in secret veins.
When therefore the physician sees that the patient feels pain, and if it is evident to him that the ailment appears to the eye, as we have said, then he must bind over that part above and below quickly: and dry above that part until the swelling subsides: and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your eyes: then care for it with the exclusion of the body, and with that which ejects the swellings, and calm it with poultices of rue, and of saffron, and the like.

Sagittae are varied according to their kinds and the places from which they are extruded from the body.
And their variety is such: because some of them have large teeth and few: and some are concave and others are convex And there are those which have three angles. And those which have tongues And those which have stars
As for those which are according to the members from which they are extruded, it is according to two kinds Either the members are from the main bodies which are concave, such as the brain, and the heart, and the spleen, and the lung, and the kidneys, and the intestines, and the bladder, and things of that kind When therefore it falls into any of these members, and a sign of death appears to you, which I will tell you afterwards, then it is necessary to extract that arrow from the body, for death follows the wound in many arrangements And when these signs do not appear, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the limb, then extract it, and care for the wound.
When these signs do not appear, and the arrow has not penetrated the bone, then extract it and care for the wound. And perhaps blood will flow from the nail or from the flesh. And perhaps the flesh will be severed and the bone will be broken And from the wound will come out a white moisture like pus And from it will come out something like the moisture of meat
If these signs appear, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of an arrow that has entered the cord are: that it is close to the left breast: and that it feels as though it is fixed in something hard, not in something soft And perhaps the arrow will move like the pulse: and blood will flow from the wound. And following this will be cold extremity, and cold sweat, and fainting You know therefore that death approaches with doubt
The sign of an arrow that has pierced the lung is the exit of bloody foam from the wound: and the breath that follows the head becomes thick and changes color like that of the sick person He expires greatly and with difficulty: and he seeks the attraction of cold air
If the arrow falls into the chest, that is near the ribs: then there is great pain with breathing, with sighing: and all the limbs of the arms move violently
If an arrow should fall into the stomach, it may possibly emerge from the wound through some undigested food.
If an arrow should fall into the belly and settle there: something of stool or vomit or intestine which has been ruptured will issue from the wound. There is no hope for its cure, neither in the extraction of the arrow.
If an arrow falls into the bladder and comes out with urine: and something proceeds from it outwards, and the pain is more severe: then you know that it is deadly
As for the rest of the members, such as the face, and the neck, and the gut, and the spatula, and the attendant, and the rib cage, and the backbone, and the shoulder, and the hip, and the thigh, and the like of these members, if they do not find the arrow in the artery or nerve: and if there is no venomous arrow.
I tell you, concerning these arrows, that I have heard of none of them, except as a sign of your desperation concerning your cure.
That is because I have heard that an arrow once fell into the tear-duct of his eye, or in the root of his nose. I extracted it from the other side, under the earwax of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm remained in his eye
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen into the fat of his eye under his lower eyelid: and the arrow was hidden, and I did not find its tip until I had cut through its wood: and it was a large arrow, such as those which the Turks shoot with their bows, with lead in it, in which there are no ears
He was healed of this, and no harm befell him in his eye
And I extracted another sausage from the Christian's belly.
And it was an Arab sausage, which is like that which has ears. And so I went above and beyond two ribs: and it was submerged in his belly. I was careful with it and brought it out: and the Christian was healed
And I extracted another sausage which had burst inside him: and we thought that it would die from it.
After that, for thirty days, nothing changed in his condition concerning the sausage: and I saw that it had healed, and I extracted it: and he was healed, and nothing bad happened to him.
And I saw the man from whom the sausage had burst in his back, and the wound was closed with a bandage over the sausage And after seven years, the sausage was extracted from the root of his hip bone
And I saw a woman whose sausage had burst inside her: and the wound was closed with a bandage over the sausage, and the sausage remained inside her: and no change occurred in her condition from her natural operations
And I saw another man whose sausage had burst on his face: and it was closed with a bandage, and it remained, without causing him much pain: and similar things happened to many people
And I extracted a sausage from the belly of the King, which had burst in the middle of his nose, and it had declined to the right side: and the sausage was completely hidden
I was called to care for him after the bursting of the sausage until three days later I found the arrow stuck quite tightly in the flesh.
I grasped it with a fine hook, and did not feel it: and indeed I found the point of the arrow and the pain from its side on the right hand I supposed that it was the point of the arrow at the extremity of the arrow.
I plastered the place with a plaster which was moist and sticky, confident that the place would be relaxed, and that I would see the sign of the arrow, that is, that it would leave its mark above the wound: and this did not happen in the place where the arrow had followed the place of the wound.
I remained with the plaster on for many days: and it did not happen that anything occurred, and the arrow remained stuck in the meantime: and he remained sick from the extraction of it until he felt the arrow one day enter his nose. And he told me this.
And he told me this. I put on him a sharp, corrosive medicine on that place for many days: until it opened: and when I grasped it with my hook, and I felt the extremity of the arrow slightly which adhered to the wood.
And I added in the opening of the wound by that sharp medicine until we saw the extremity of the arrow clearly: and we passed it out with it in that space of time for about four months.
And when the wound had healed, and it was possible for me to introduce forceps into it, and to move it, and it did not obey the coming out: then I did not cease to grasp it with a fine hook, and to fashion above it with the wisdom of experienced men, until I was able to grasp the arrow with forceps at the end, and I drew it out Then I restored the limb.
And the physicians say that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I restored it therefore itself, and the limb was consolidated, and the illness was healed completely: nothing was left of it
And I tell thee the quality of the extraction of some arrows: so that thou mayest know the rule and the signification of that which I have not said
For the parts of this art and the distinctions of them are not comprehended with the narration: neither doth this book comprehend them, and a prudent artificer compares them with little to much: and with that which is present, with that which is absent: and he finds a new operation and an new instrument among the descent of the things of this art when they happen.
I say therefore that arrows do not go out of the limbs in which they are fixed, except in two ways. Either by attraction from the place from which it came in. Or by turning back the other part
And that which goes out from where it came in, or the arrow proceeding in the fleshy place: it is attracted and goes out.
If it does not obey the going out at the hour in which it fell: then it is necessary that thou shouldest let it go a few days, until the flesh which is around it is putrefied: and the attraction of it and its going out will be removed
And in the same way if it is caught in a bone, and it does not obey thee at the going out, then thou shouldest also let it go a few days: and repeat it every day with the attraction and the motion, until it goes out
If it does not obey thee at the going out after days, then it is necessary that thou shouldest perforate round about the arrow in the bone from all sides with a fine thread, until the arrow be amplified Then draw out the abscess, and extract it.

Of the phlebotomy of the two veins after the Golden Age
Phlebotomy of the two veins which are after the Golden Age is useful for ancient cataracts, headache, haemorrhoids, and a bad headache of the head.
And what is the quality of a phlebotomist according to what I have said? And it is, as I have said, that the phlebotomist should cause the head of the patient to bleed: and he should grasp the head of the patient at the two veins near the rough cloth with good friction. Then he should tighten the patient's neck with a cloth, until two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head Ask therefore easily with your finger, and where you feel the pulse of the veins under your finger, there will be a sign with the cautery.
Then take the phlebotomist's knife, which is called Incidens. Then insert it under the vein into the skin, until the phlebotomist reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin towards the upper part, and cut the vein with the incision of the phlebotomist. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers, or thereabouts: and let blood flow out as much as you wish
Then tie up the wound with threads, and let go of the wound, until it is healed.
And thus it is in the beginning of the book that the incision of the ears and their cautery are mentioned

However, three veins are usually phlebotomized in the elbow
And their phlebotomy is done in two ways. Either by grasping with a large needle: or by lancing with an awl to the point of slight bleeding: or by cutting with a knife, which is called Alnesil. This is its form
There are broad and narrow species according to the width of the veins and the tension of them. The form of phlebotomy with the large needle is broad, like oil It is suitable for opening wide, full, protruding, apparent, large, and those containing thick blood
This phlebotomus is of smaller width and finer extremities.
It is suitable for the phlebotomy of fine veins which contain thin yellow blood.
Basiliae, however, which is one of the three veins mentioned for phlebotomy, is an instrument. Because it draws blood from the swellings which are under the jaw and the collar, from the part following the chest and the groin: and it is suitable for the phlebotomist because of its resemblance to the phlebotomus of the veins, so that it should not cause fear that there is an artery beneath it If, therefore, it err, and the puncture of the phlebotomy should happen, the artery will be injured, and the flow of blood will be interrupted Therefore it is necessary not to perform phlebotomy with the phlebotomus at the same time as the puncture: but rather let it dry with Alnesil.
If, however, Basilica does not appear visibly, then it is necessary to seek it elsewhere: either by means of other veins or by inquiry of another person; or to perform the phlebotomy in another place, such as the elbow vein For it is itself evident, and should be left dry with the Anesil, as we have already said.

And let the blow not fall outside the space of the artery.

And do not move him for a few days, until he is healed.

Of the cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is an instrument, as it draws blood from the head and supplies it to the eyes.
It is necessary in this vein alone, if you will, to phlebotomize with the olive-leaf needle, or with the Myrtle needle: because it is safer for the veins of all, because there is neither artery nor nerve under it
Indeed it is convenient to you in phlebotomy to ask for the soft parts [of the head] with the first blow, and to seek a softer place: and it does not harm if it is not found with the first blow, as it will return with subsequent phlebotomies.
Perhaps the swelling may disappear in some individuals when the percussion does not take place at the first attempt, but it does not harm them in any way.
Regarding the use of phlebotomy and precautions to be taken in its performance. The quality of phlebotomy and the accidents associated with it, concerning which you must reflect
Firstly, know that phlebotomy is not to be undertaken except for the preservation of health, and for the cessation of ailments, and for the prevention of diseases.
It is to be performed in the cubital veins, that is the Cephalica, and the Fusca, and the Basilica. It should be performed in the spring after Lent, in the summer, and in the autumn, and in the winter after meals, when there are signs of fullness
It should be performed on the first or third day after the lapse of three hours
Phlebotomy for diseases has no fixed time: but when it is necessary and convenient, day or night, at any hour and at any time.
It should not be performed in children until they are more than fourteen years of age Neither should it be performed in a pregnant woman except from the fourth month until the seventh month Nor in an elderly person who has passed the age of sixty years
When anyone wishes to undergo phlebotomy for whatever reason: it is necessary to administer a laxative before phlebotomy if there is much stool retained in the intestines: in order that they do not adhere to the main veins during phlebotomy, causing harm to the body
Neither the patient's vomiting nor the nausea itself should be forced, neither by pressure nor from drunkenness, until the cause of the former has vanished from the body. Neither with hydrophobia shall the patient be left
And let another bloodletting be performed after the colic passion, vomiting, diarrhea, frequent urination, sexual abstinence, rest, exercise, joy, and fasting, which dissolve strength from the body or the animal
Then consider the sublimity of the humors before that, if blood is thick with food and drink, and with physicians: if it is possible for him.
Then let a bath be introduced, if it does not forbid it: or let some exercise be performed, in order to temper the blood.
And let a phlebotomy be made of him in the chest, about the third day: as we have said: and endeavor that he evacuate his chest from all these bad things: such as envy, wrath, fear: and from all bodily things, such as labor, suffering, and superabundance, and such like these.
And make his food be present with things which are customarily used by men from fragrance, and odor, and delight, and the like, as much as is possible
Then let the phlebotomist sit above the patient's heel, which is higher than the heel of the phlebotomist: then he shall extract his arm and wipe it with his hand twice, or three times. Then tie the ligament with a bandage, and wrap it twice around the limb twice.
And let the restraint be temperate, for when the restraint is unequal, or through excessive restraint, then it hinders the course of the blood: or in softening, then also it hinders the course of the blood
Then after the stricture has been passed, it is necessary that the phlebotomist should immediately seize both hands together and hold them close to the belly, until the veins have filled up and the sense has returned.
Then let the phlebotomist take hold of the vein with a slight grasp, as before.</start_of_turn>
Then he places his finger on the index finger of the left hand over the vein at the place where he wishes to make the phlebotomy, slightly: so that the vein does not slip away, and that the percussion is set.</start_of_turn For from the veins is more to be feared like a flying cord during phlebotomy: and from the blood that is filled with air: and when you place the phlebotomy on the vein, it contracts under the phlebotomy, and the phlebotomist is struck, and the vein is not opened by the phlebotomy: and if it is opened, it is a stricture: because of this, it is necessary that the phlebotomist should be firm and attentive in all these things.</start_of_turn
Then he lets go of the phlebotomy.</start_of_turn If thus the vein opens from the blood, then it is good.</start_of_turn And if not, then repeat it with another vein nearby, or above that with force: if the place does not recover.</start_of_turn

I say, therefore, when any fracture, dislocation, twist, or fall occurs: then it is necessary at once to apply phlebotomy and solve it, or both together: if nothing else hinders, such as weakness of spirit: or is he who has done it to him a boy, or an old man decrepitus: or is the time urgent of heat, or urgent of cold.

When the bone is beginning to heal, then it is necessary, that the patient should eat nourishing and bulky foods, such as barley bread cooked with water, barley meal, heads, feet, beans, fat, fish: and strong drink, and things like these. With this regimen the coagulation of the fracture will be faster and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in the joints which are in their first old age, and decrepit, it is not possible that they should continue and consolidate according to their natural course always, because of the wasting away of the limbs: and they consolidate and continue only what is in the end of leniency, like bones of the newborn calf
But nature adds upon the broken bone from all sides something like glue, which is of great thickness, which adheres and binds it, until they are congealed together, and bound together, until it comes in the end to strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until it is not hindered by any operation: and for this reason it is necessary that you should give the patient nourishing food, which has abundance, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said
And know that there are various kinds of fractures, according to the diversity of the limbs: for as the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the head: and the fracture of the breastbone is different from the fracture of the back: and in the same way all the other limbs are different from each other: and I come with the remembrance of the kind of fracture which is exposed in his chapter distinguished from the others. Diverse forms of bone fracture also exist within themselves.
For indeed, such fractures are arched, apart from the fact that they happen alongside splinters. And when a fracture happens along the length of the bone: such fractures are of the saddle type, that is, splinters, and protrusions, like those in the quill, when the blade is applied to write Not from that: and it is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head: and it is a small fissure.
And every kind has its own wisdom, or its own instrument for restoration, according to the memory of its restoration, distinguished in its places.
From which it is known that a fracture of the bone is its tortuousness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand
When there is no apparent tortuousness in the place, nor any sound on touching the bone with your hand, nor does the patient experience great pain: then it is not a fracture, but it is possible that there is only a slight fracture or a slight displacement: it is not therefore necessary to move the limb with the hand and compress it completely, but rather to place above it the hands of the physicians who know the remedy, after which the tightening should be done subtly
Know that when an arc fractures, and breaks into two, apart from the splinters that happen alongside, the whole limb is really moved from its place: then you must start from the beginning to restore it, equating it before the swelling becomes cold
But if swelling occurs, then defer it for a few days, until the swelling subsides. Then, equalize it according to whatever degree you wish with lenity and ingenuity.

Of the treatment of an accident in the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and their figures are many: and their causes are distinct.
For from a fracture is that which is made by an impact with a weapon: and the impact raises the whole bone until it reaches the marrow, which is under the bone, like an axis in wood: and for this reason this species of fracture is called Axial.
If any bone is fractured and breaks the face of the bone, but does not penetrate to the innermost part of the bone: this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, which is to say, complete fracture.
It is a common fracture, either great or small.</start_of_turn>
A fracture is a break, either complete or incomplete. The cause of this is impact with a stone, or a fall from a height, or something similar.</start_of_turn>
This kind of fracture is also either penetrating or non-penetrating. The penetrating fracture approaches the bone marrow, either on the face of the bone or in its substance. This is a serious fracture, either great or small.</start_of_turn>
There is also a fracture which is hidden in the substance of the bone, under the layer of hair. This is a small fracture and is called a Capillary fracture.</start_of_turn>
There is also a fracture which occurs when a blow or impact causes the lamina of the bone to break inwards, and causes concavity. This is like what happens with blows of the wind, when something blows inwards.</start_of_turn>
This mostly happens in the heads of soft bones, such as the heads of young children.</start_of_turn>
All these different kinds of fractures are separated from each other, and I will mention them all in their place.</start_of_turn>
We recognize all these kinds of fractures by their appearance on the surface, their location, and the loosening of the corrupted flesh above them.</start_of_turn
The Capillary fracture is recognized by the fact that it appears on the bone, is stripped away, and is covered with ointment: the fracture appears black.</start_of_turn
In treating fractures, consider first the signs of the sick person: then, if you are of the opinion that these signs signify something manifest over fear, such as vomiting of bile, and swelling, and loss of intellect, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and discharge from the eyes, and redness of the face, and similar things which are in many conditions, it is in vain to expect recovery unless you treat these signs. If you do not consider these signs to be serious, and you hope for recovery, then begin to treat him.
If a wound comes to you in winter at the beginning of the month, then it is necessary to study the evacuation of bone before the fourteenth day of the month in every case.
If it is in the summer days, then it is necessary to be careful to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it becomes corrupted under the membrane, and these things happen in many cases.</start_of_turn>
If a fracture of a bone has reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is small and accompanied by traction: then it is necessary to cut away the broken part and the traction, as I have told you.</start_of_turn>
And it is necessary to reduce the head of the wounded person: and determine over the bone in whatever way is possible: and according to the figure of the wound and what is useful for the patient.</start_of_turn
If it happens to you at the time of detecting a flowing of blood or a hardened abscess: then oppose it with that which is necessary.</start_of_turn And it is necessary to place the place with soaked cloths in wine, and wet sponges: and make the wound quiet, until the abscess settles, and be secure from the flow of blood.</start_of_turn> Then commence with the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to two methods.
The first method is as follows: you cut the bone with a curved chisel and a straight chisel: this is its form
Then give the chisel another blow after the wide chisel has been applied: this is its form
And give another blow with the wide chisel yet again.
And the second method is as follows: you have several chisels of different sizes: some are wider than others, and some are narrower than others: and their tips should be sharp and made of iron or lead: and use them easily on the chisel, so that you do not break the head and damage it
If, however, the bone is strong and hard: then it is necessary to perforate its circumference before you apply the chisels, with a needle that is not too deep: and it is not called by that name, except when it does not pass beyond the limit of bone cartilage to that which is after it: for the needle is the rounded extremity above that, which is under its sharp point, like the margin, and a small circle prevents it from submerging, and from passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting that you take many needles of these, whose number is appropriate for the thickness of the bone, until they are all above the bone: whose acute extremities in length and breadth are in proportion to the thickness of that bone
And this is the form of the three species of needles, or trepanas.
The quality of the perforation in the circumference of the fractured bone is: to place the needle on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been pierced Then move the bone to another place, and put between every hole the amount of breadth of the radius, or somewhat less.
Then cut with the incisors between every hole of the bone: make it thinner than the thickness of the bone at its thickest point, as we have said, until you have cut through the bone, or your hand, or with any other instrument which you have at your disposal: such as tweezers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary to take care that you do not touch the bone, or any instrument.
When you have surrounded the bone with the perforation, and it is separated from the surrounding tissue, if it is still connected with anything, grasp it with another instrument similar to the incisor and pull it out. But it is necessary to make it thin and smooth compared with other instruments.
If there remain any small bones and fragments of bone: grasp them easily with the instrument which is beside you Then care for the wound afterwards as we have mentioned, and with the unguents which we mentioned earlier.
And another way of performing the operation is easy, far from fear: which Galen praised highly, and which is his own saying. It is not necessary at first to remove the part of the bone which is more fractured and prominent: until you have removed that part and placed under it the tip of this lens-shaped incisor which has this shape
The tip of the lens does not penetrate anything: and the acute part of its edge is wide on its two sides in length, like a wedge: so that the lens-shaped part is close to the surrounding tissue, making the acute part of its edge on the bone
Then strike above the Incisorium from one side with a small hammer until you have pierced the whole bone easily, as it will recoil: and you will be safe from the nail, which does not happen in this case as you fear beforehand.
Even if the physician is not stupid among men, and is more ignorant of this than anyone, or if he is asleep, then if anything remains attached to the bone from the nail in some place, he will pierce it from there with the lens of the incisor itself and will liberate it easily. It is liberated from it without pain, and without fear.
And if the bone is not pierced through to the nail, and that which is broken off from the bone is its face, and remains in it rough and spiky: then it is necessary to remove that roughness, and to grind away those spines with fine files of various quantities, so that it is possible to administer from them in every place where it is convenient, and to make them straight, according to what the operation leads you to, and the fractured bone itself, and its figure And it is necessary to administer in your reason, that in the first place you should radiate the larger files, and then the smaller ones. Then use the finer one afterwards.
And it is necessary to administer in your reason, that in the first place you should radiate the larger files, and then the smaller ones. Then use the finer one afterwards. And do not cease doing this until you have reached the finest, and most delicate of them all
With every little fracture remaining of small hairs, and the fracture itself being delicate, it is necessary to take care, according to what leads to its rectification.
And it is a thing which is not hidden over everything, to which in this art there is anything studious, and which stands over that which we have written, and which we have shown in great fractures
If the wound persists after you have closed the bones: you must take up a piece of lint, according to the size of the wound, and moisten it in oil of roses, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another piece of lint, doubled or tripled, and moisten it in wine and oil of roses, and place it over the first piece: and make it as easy as possible to bear on it, so that it does not swell up
Then bandage the surrounding area: and do not tighten it, except for the amount that will hold the wound only: and remove it when the wound is secure from the heat of the afterburn. Then wash it and give it the necessary medicines which are appropriate for such a case: such as the roots of lily, and the flour of herbs, and barley flour, and marigold, and the like of these: and make from these medicines a powder, which shall be sprinkled over the wound as though it were dry powder.
And in general give in that all medicines that have the property of draining and not biting And take care according to your experience, so that the wound may be soft in your care: neither greasy nor dry bloodstains.
And take care according to your experience, so that the wound may be soft in your care: neither greasy nor dry bloodstains. Neither let blood clots form in it in any way: because when blood clots form over the wound of the brain, they corrupt it and cause it to putrefy, and this brings about great mischief: and this happens in the swelling of the head, when it is neglected and its care is omitted, black spots appear on its surface.
Consider therefore now, if such serious accidents happen to you, you know that it is dangerous to delay treatment.
If the blackness does not depart, except for the medicine which is put upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to make it black: then it is necessary, that you take from the honey one part, from the rose-water three parts, and lines as many as there are grains of sand. Then put it upon the wound: afterwards take care of it with the means which are suitable, until it is healed.
When the nose is fractured, restoration must be delayed
You know that the nose is not fractured except for two of its upper parts together, or one of them: because they are two bones: for the lower one of them is cartilaginous and does not fracture, and does not depart except for attrition, and twisting, and simulation
If one of the two parts is fractured, then it is necessary to insert the little finger into the hole of the nose, and to equal the fracture with the index finger and the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and do this with skill and ease. Take care, that you do not cause further pain by your operation.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and does not yield to the insertion of the finger: then it is necessary to equalize the thickness of the radius at the small curvature
If there is a fracture in both parts, then do the same: and be careful with the restoration in the first day of the fracture, if possible If not, then after the seventh or tenth day from the setting of the wound.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a strip of linen from the cloth of the bandage, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two strips if the fracture is in both parts: and let the strip be of such a thickness as to fill the hole of the nose
Some of the ancient restorers have said that they infuse gum into butter and change it every day. It does not seem to me that this is necessary; indeed, it would be better to infuse gum into barley flour mixed with fine powder of bread Then remove the gum until the bone is firm, and apply ointment.
When the gum has been introduced into the nose in the place of the sponge of an ointment pen, after it has been covered with a soft cushion: then the fracture of the nose is held more firmly, and prevents the sickly person from being stifled by inspiration.
This is not necessary. If you wish, do so; if not, do the gum.
If it happens that the nose is again fractured while you are working after the swelling has subsided, plaster the nose with plaster, or with burnt ochre mixed with fat, or with some other salve.
If the swelling has subsided, then it is necessary to plaster it with wheat flour mixed with burnt barley flour and butter of almonds Then place over it a soft cushion, and do not bind the nose with anything whatsoever.
If indeed the bones of the nose are fractured by a slight fracture or bruising, then it is necessary to split them apart with instruments that are suitable for this purpose and extract them Then cut them apart, and care for the wound with what is necessary, and consolidate with suitable bandages or unguents.
If there is a nail or splinter in the nose, then it is necessary to remove it with sponges: and give him a lead weight until it is healed
On restoration of the lower jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured and its fracture is not associated with a wound: then consider if its fracture is only lateral, and not fractured into two pieces, and that it is directed inwards: then its knowledge is easy.
It is necessary therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, to insert the index finger of the left hand into the bone of the patient: similarly if the fracture is on the left side, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the bulge of the fracture inwards with ease along with the other hand from the inside outwards, and with the same hand from the outside inwards
If the fracture of the lower jaw has already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to manage the expansion from both parts, according to their direction: until their equilibrium is possible
If however there is displacement in the teeth, or rupture: then bind those from which you hope that which remain with a thread, or silver, or silk. Then put over the fractured jaw a cushion. Then put over it a doubled pad, and put over the pad a large sponge made hollow: or a piece of the sole of a shoe of equal length to the mandible.
Then tie it up above, according to what is prepared for its ligation, and agree with its application, until it is not destroyed: and command the patient to rest in peace and quiet: and give him food of soft consistency.
If however you estimate that something has changed with the figure or mode: then be careful, and solve it on the third day Then mend what has been altered, and plaster it with fine flour of wheat, with alum or with sesame flour, after you have removed the tumor: and put on a layer of soft wool over the plaster.
While that plaster remains attached to that, and does not change the position of the bone, remove it, do not dissolve it, until it has healed: or bind the fracture.</start_of_turn> Many times this fracture is bound for seven days.</start_of_turn>
If in the meantime a callous swelling should happen, then manage it as we said before until that swelling has subsided.</start_of_turn
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider it.</start_of_turn> For if it is separated from the bone fragment, or from the satay: then be careful in this, that you do not increase the fracture with those instruments.</start_of_turn
If indeed the opening of the wound is strict, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the quantity of necessity.</start_of_turn
Then when you have removed that fragment of satay, and nothing remains of it, seal up the opening of the wound, if it is wide: and if not, put on over it the necessary plaster, and bind it, until it has healed.</start_of_turn
On restoration of the Furcula when it is fractured.</start_of_turn
Indeed, the Furcula is most frequently fractured from the front part towards the humerus, and the fracture of it is done in one of three ways.</start_of_turn
Or it is fractured and separated into two, besides which they happen in those satayes: and that is easier for its restoration.</start_of_turn
Or thus, what happens in the case of a fracture of the bone of the thigh: it is more difficult to restore.
Or thus, when there is a fracture with an ulcer.
And the operation in such case when there is a fracture without an ulcer is that you must keep two assistants, one of whom holds the fractured fragment in place, and the other extends the limb above the fractured part.
Then equalize the fracture of the fingers of your hand, until it has attained its proper shape, as it should be: and let it not be prominent, neither hollow
If, however, it does not straighten out by extension alone, then it is necessary to place under the knee of the patient a ball of wool or felt: and let the size of it be according to the quantity of necessity. And extend and elevate the fractured limb, and cover the ball with the hand, until the fracture is equal, as it should be
If it is not possible to pull the limb of the fractured bone outwards: because it has gone deep: then it is necessary to recline the patient upon his chest: and place under the elbow of him the middle finger of his hand in size, and keep his arm minister to the lower part: until the bone of the fractured limb which is in the depth has been raised up: and then straighten the fracture and equalize the fingers of your hand
If, however, you feel swelling from the fracture, and it moves: then it is necessary to find above that and extract it easily.
If, however, it is retained in the bone, then make an incision in it with one of your surgical instruments which you have prepared for this purpose: after you have placed under the fractured limb an instrument which will hold the bone in place. And the instrument is of wood, or of iron. This is its form.
It is like a cochlea which has no cavity
Its breadth is according to that from which it proceeds from the magnitude of the bone, and its height/width is small. Its length is also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it has two extremities like a pair of tongs: one extremity is wide and the other is narrow
If, however, you tear the wound when you extract the clot from the bone, it is wide, and secure it from the heat of the afterburn: then join together the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the tear is small, or the afterburn is slight: then close the wound with pieces of lint, and with sponges according to the quantity of the wound
If there is afterburn, then pour oil of roses, aceto, and wine over the wound and put on top of it.
The stiffness of the bone, however, when there is no wound and no section, is to put over the bone a plaster made of ground barley with almonds or eggshell. And put on a soft cushion over it. And put under the head a sphere if you do not digest it Then take a sponge, which is like the one which the Saracens hold, which is long and wide: and its breadth is like that of a palm of the hand, or somewhat less And put a doubled sponge over the cushion and the plaster. And take a needle from a thin board, whose breadth is three fingers wide, and in length like that Then immerse the wound in the cloth. Then immerse the wound in the cloth covering the fractured area that is above the break and the surrounding area. Then place the bandage over the fracture as if rolling it up, and immerse the wound above the collar of the healthy side and below the collar of the diseased side: and reduce the wound over all parts, and as much as the dressing holds the fracture steady: and this is what is not visible from above, and all, so that nothing is disturbed from the fracture itself. And for this reason it is necessary to wash the diseased area every day. Whenever the ligament is softened and the dressing is loosened: then rectify that and tighten the ligament
Then place the patient to sleep on their back, and place under their chin the small pillows in the night, so that when they are raised up their attendants will support them on their side. The fracture of the collarbone is elevated by elevating the arm
Or bind the arm to the chest: and do not loosen the ligament unless a wound occurs from itching or from a swelling, until twelve days have passed
Then renew the dressing if you see its face to be wet And repeat the binding, and loosen it, until it is restored and coagulated.
And the fracture of the collarbone is bound and comforted according to many for eighty days: and in some people in less time
Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the collarbone.
Rarely does the spatula break, and in its wide place: and they do not break from its margins.
When it breaks from that place, and breaks in its middle, and it is not known except by touch: then it is necessary to prepare it according to the form of the fracture. And reduce it in accordance with the natural form in every way that is possible.
Then put over the place fine powder of mill with alum, and a soft cushion, and put above it a double layer of cloth. Then put over it a wide bandage from a thin board according to the quantity of the spatula, or a little more.
If indeed under the bandage there is a concavity in some place of the spatula, then equalize that concavity with a soft cushion until the bandage descends evenly
Then bind it up again with long silk, gentle tension, and firmness of the bandage, until it does not move from its place.
And consider the ligament every day. Whenever it is softened, string it up, and equalize the bandage if it moves from its place: and let the patient lie on his sound side
And the spatula is restored in ninety days, or in ninety-five days.
When this number is completed, then loosen the ligament: and you will be secure For it is from the bones whose communication does not change, and whose solution does not take long
If however it proceeds from a broken bone, and protrudes under the skin: then split over it, and remove it.</start_of_turn> And do as we have said concerning the fracture of the furcula from the sedation of the apostematis calidi, if anything be added to it.

When it fractures in the middle, which inclines downwards, and there is pain, difficulty in swallowing, and cough: and perhaps blood is omitted, and there is a bulge in the fractured bone, which is not hidden from sense. The restoration of it is that the invalid be placed upon his back: and put between his shoulder blades a cushion.</start_of_turn
Then press his arms, and bind his ribs from two parts or from two sides with hands: and twist them in its equilibrium, according to every possible way which can be done for it and which agrees, until the shape of the bone is restored, as it should be.</start_of_turn
