When the bones are fractured, restore them.
You know that a fracture of the bones does not happen except in the great places which follow the backbone, and from the extremities of them from the anterior part And does not happen except for this, because they are cartilaginous And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense in the examination with the fingers
And its restoration is done in such a way that it makes the fracture even with the fingers as much as possible, until the figure is equal, as it should be Then the dressing, and bind the fractured bone with a pin if necessary.
And if the fracture of the bones is downward towards the inside: then it happens with great pain which is unbearable, and swelling, as in those who have pleurisis: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also it happens that there is difficulty with breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of blood: and this is a difficult healing.
And the ancients were skilled in this with many skills: and among them are those who say that you should put on bandages the sick who cause inflammation and swelling, so that the belly inflates, and expands, and drives out the fracture towards the outside.
We, however, abominate that it should be a cause of making the swelling worse, if it does not happen. If however it happens, then join in it.
And some of them say: put on over the swelling place, then draw it together with force, and this is similar to the calculation Truth be told, when moist substances are drawn towards a location, it is due to the disposition of the disabled.
Some have said: It is necessary for the place to cooperate with the wool which is already submerged in warm oil: and they put in feathers between the ribs until it is firm: so that there is an even ligation when you roll up the belly according to its roundness.
Then one must care for the wounded man with food and medicine.
If one binds up a patient too tightly with that which he cannot tolerate, and if the bone is pierced with a wound that is gaping, and we fear that the patient will die: then it is necessary to split open the place and remove the fractured rib bone Then we put over this an instrument which preserves the fat, whose memory has gone before: and we break the bone easily and break through the bone itself
Then we join together two lips of the wound, if there is a large one with a suture: and we care for it with poultices until it heals.
If in the meantime a swelling becomes hot, then cool it down, and pour pultices of rose oil over the place: and put on it: and care for the patient with what agrees with the swelling.
Even between times he rests on his side over which he sleeps, until he heals
On the restoration of fractures of the rib and neck bones.
The bones of the neck when they are fractured sometimes happen by attrition: and in the same way the rib bones also. When this happens to anyone, and you do not know whether he will heal or not: then keep quiet If you aid the hand when swollen, stiff, and dead, it will not move, neither extend nor contract: and when you compress it with your fingers and needles with an awl, you do not feel pain, neither do you feel it in its insides. Then you know that it is not healing, and that it is deadly.
When it moves, and you feel constriction in its insides with your fingers and needle, then you know that the bone is broken, and that it will heal only with treatment.
If you bring close to each other the pain of the back like that of the hand, and you do not know whether it will heal or not: then look at its feet. If you aid what is softened in the hands and comes into this in the back: then when it lies over its belly, there comes out swelling and discharge without its will. And when it lies over its belly and wishes to move, and it cannot: then you know that it is deadly. It is of no use to work on its cure.
If indeed nothing happens to it from this, then it is easier.
Its cure from this is that you bring near its afterburn with the warmth of oil of roses: thus, put on the back what is attracted to the Spondila with olive oil or balm.
Put on it at every other time in the day until when the afterburn has subsided: then put on one bandage over the place and cover it with a soft dressing, and bind it up with a thread: and command the patient to rest quietly and peacefully Let him not sleep over the part where he has pain until it has healed
If anything happens at the attrition of the bone of the thigh, or if anything is separated from it: then it is necessary to cut above the skin and raise it up. Then join together two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture.</start_of_turn> Then care for it with some nourishment, from unguents, until it heals.</start_of_turn>
If indeed the thigh bone is fractured behind and the end of the leg is hanging: then it is necessary to insert the little finger of the left hand into the buttock, and equalize the fractured bone in the hand, according to what is possible, and with the second hand.</start_of_turn>
Then place above it [the bone] and elevate it: and care for the wound with the care that went before, until it heals.</start_of_turn

Rarely are the bones of the limb fractured.</start_of_turn If they are fractured, then the fracture of them is not, except for a dislocation in the extremities: and they are torn along their length, and bend inwards: and there results mild pain in the place, and swelling: and the leg that is fractured becomes stiff.</start_of_turn

Of the restoration of the fracture.
The aid is that which is between the elbow up to the head of the scapula.</start_of_turn>
If indeed it is fractured, then the restoration of it is according to one of two methods.</start_of_turn
One of them is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this shape.</start_of_turn
And two ligatures are bound at the two extremities.</start_of_turn
Then it is suspended from a high place: and the patient sits upon a seat.</start_of_turn Then he will project his fractured arm over the wood until it reaches the elbow of his limb, in which middle there is an attached fragment of bone.
Then he will suspend something weighty above it, so as to extend the limb downwards. Then the physician will stabilize the fracture with his hands, until the fracture has healed, as required
And another method is that the patient should rest on his bed: and suspend his arm from his neck with a ligament Then command two assistants, that one should hold that which is above the fracture with his hands, and that the other should hold the lower one, and that each of them should extend his to his part.
And if desired, in order that the extension may be stronger, then bind under the fracture with a ligament and above it with another ligament: and extend both of them by two assistants to their two parts.
If the fracture is close to the elbow of the arm, then it is necessary to put on one ligament under the elbow and another under the fracture above the elbow
And in the same way if the fracture is close to the forearm: then it is necessary to put on a ligament over the place of the wound, and over the forearm itself.
Then stabilize the fracture easily without toil, until the fracture has healed according to what is required, and apply a good dressing: then bind it up, if it has not already gone cold
If indeed a cold swelling has occurred, then remove the bandage until the seventh day. And put upon it a soaked bandage in vinegar and salt, until the swelling has subsided: then bind it up: and the way of binding it up is that you put on a plaster on the fracture.
Then place a new patch over the wound. Then take hold of the limb with your arm, and open your hand over the shoulder bone.
Put on a bandage, and bind the limb with your arm so that the limb is fixed in place without hindering you from anything or causing you any pain from the fractured bone
If at any time you notice something wrong with the fractured bone, put over it the larger and stronger bandage of the two And place between every bandage a width equal to the breadth of a finger. And let the length of the bandages be according to the fracture plus three fingers from all sides
Then bind over the bandages as I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And let your binding be tighter over the site of the fracture The more prolonged the fracture, the tighter the binding should be
If therefore you observe the position of the bandages and the binding at the time you restore the limb, do this: and if you notice swelling, loosen the binding and the bandages until the seventh day, as we have said before
Then consider the ligament in every three days, so that no pain, swelling or impediment to the passage of food to the limb occurs due to the tension of the binding Do all this, as we have said before
If you are in doubt, do not loosen the ligament before three days, or more
Let the patient rest with their head on their chest, and their hand on their stomach: and place under the support a full cushion of wool equally.
Consider at all times of night and day that the figure of the fractured limb should not be allowed to become loose, or the ligament should not be softened. The limb should be set firmly in its entirety for your entire care
And provide the patient with food in accordance with the order we have given, so that it be light in the beginning, until the bone begins to knit: then it is necessary that you increase the amount of food gradually.
As for the attendance of the attendants and the patient, it is necessary that the limb be bound for forty days: then it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath should be administered, and that unguents be applied which will rectify this
If the fracture be dirty or raw: then do not loosen the ligament or the fragments until fifty days have passed, and until two months have elapsed.
Regarding the restoration of a fractured limb.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are named the two ulnae.
One of them is the forearm: and that which follows the thumb The other is the forearm, and that which is placed under the forearm from the lower part
Perhaps therefore the large bone is fractured only, or the forearm: and perhaps both together
When therefore the upper forearm bone is fractured, then the restoration of the same is easy, and the healing is more rapid
And when the lower forearm bone is fractured, the fracture is bad, and the healing is difficult
And it is worse when both bones are fractured together
If the bone which is fractured is slightly above the navel, then the physician must be present at the restoration of it, in order to place the extension of the gut with ease, until it equals itself.
If the fracture is of a large bone: then it is necessary to place a stronger support around it
It is necessary to place the hand figure at the restoration of it and to extend the limb beyond the elbow The thumb should be placed above the wrist of all fingers and the lower joint of all fingers, or of some of them: and the patient should sit square upon himself, and the elbow should be at the level of the shoulder or somewhat above it, so that the patient does not incur any pain.
Then extend the arm from below, or with the hand itself, or with the ligament. And the other limb extends in the same way from above
Then the physician must equalize the bone, until it is reduced to a better shape than before
If there are any protrusions in the fracture, then try to reduce them in their place without haste.
If there appears in it a separated piece of cartilage, which has pierced the skin, and there is no hope of restoring it: then find above it and remove it in the manner which we mentioned above
If however the fracture is with a wound, I do this for the head alone, and take care of the cure from that moment on
If at the beginning of your restoration you see a soft swelling, then draw the gut with a piece of cotton wool which has been made fine, and white, and put the middle between the thickness and the thinness: and bind the gut over it with a fine suture until the swelling has subsided: then remove the cotton wool, and put an ointment prepared from pounded wheat with albumin or oil. Then place sponges, and let the sponge which is placed over the fracture be wider and stronger.
And know that the number of sponges for the arm is six in various positions: if the fracture is in one cavity, or in two cavities simultaneously.
Then place your bandage firmly over the fracture stronger and more tightly, and a little more with the bandage above the fracture, or below it: or place a softer bandage under the previous bandage alongside it, according as the recollection of it was at the beginning of the chapter
And let there be cloths which cover the fracture, soft cloths, moist: and not hard, rough. And let the thread which is drawn from the linen itself be medium between subtle and coarse, as we have narrated.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If anything happens whose rectification is necessary, such as itching which occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to bathe the limb with warm water until the itching ceases, and then loosen the limb, until it is quiet, then repeat the bandage
If the bandage is already softened, and the bone is already removed, and things are similar to these: then rectify all this with your skill, and consider it again.
For if the nourishment is hindered from reaching the limb because of the excess of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and let it rest for a few days, until the nourishment reaches it again, then bind it again
But if no ailment happens to the sick person of the things which we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to break it off, except after eighty days or so
Then the hand of the patient is suspended at his collar. The collar is made equal, and his body is guarded from disturbing movements. Let him sleep over his back.
Know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps in eighty days. All this is according to the arrangements of the temperaments, and the arrangements of virtue
Of the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that the bones of the hand and the nodes of the fingers are rarely fractured And do not happen to them except for a great attrition.</start_of_turn>
When therefore such a fracture of the bone happens, or an attrition: then it is necessary that the patient sit square: and before him be placed a seat equal in height.</start_of_turn Then place his hand over his extended palm.</start_of_turn

And consider in your restoration, and after the setting of the wound: and oppose with it what is necessary, when anything happens of that, according to the course of its story.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is fractured many times, and it is declared by sense: because it curves inward and outward parts
And its restoration is done thus, that the ligament is sutured above the fracture, and the ligament is sutured below the fracture. And the infirm is returned to his face.
Then extend all the ligaments around the part to its side according to their equality: and this is when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
If however the fracture is close to the root of the coxae, then let the ligament be around the part of the hip so that the extension may go up: and let the other ligament be under the fracture
And thus in the same way if there is a fracture close to the knee, then let the ligament be close to the knee so that the extension may go downwards
Then the physician must fix the bone with both his hands, until he has reduced it according to the likeness of its natural figure: and join the bones with a good suture.
Then it is necessary to put on a plaster and bind it, if the limb is not restored after the fracture And if this happens in the restoration of the limb: then leave it for a few days, until the limb becomes calm, and then return to your treatment.
And the stricture of it is like this. It is necessary to cover over the fracture with a firm covering, that is, a bandage: and double it twice, or three times: and remain from that excess. Then cover the leg until the heel reaches the root of the nail.
And insert a long thread between the coxae and the leg near the knee downwards, and bring the ends of the thread from the upper part of both parts Then roll up over the leg and the coxae what is in excess from the other part
Then put on the coxae in the place of the fracture itself little nails, and put on a nail from above on the bone of the leg
Then fill the cavities between the thigh and hip with wool pads, so as to equalize the tension.
Then begin by making the tension strong from the middle above the fractured area, with three or four inversions And the more you lengthen with the ligament from the fractured place, the less tension you will have, and the softer and more gentle it will be
Then take two threads to the two extremities which you have introduced between the hip and thigh Tie them thus with both three threads that are above. Then lead both thread extremities down until you reach the heel of the foot And when you have tied both strings, also bind the extremities of the pins from the other side: so that the ligament does not slip from its place
Then release the tension above it, while it remains, so that no fracture, no swelling, nor inflation, or the like occurs to it. Which if anything should happen from this Then act diligently with its solution and restore what happened from the beginning, as we have said many times before
If there is a piece of bone that projects from the bone: then it must be leveled, if this is possible. And if not, find above it, and remove it: and take care of it until the pain has passed away
When this fracture of the hip is bound, besides the pins which are added, do as we said in the treatise and in the arm
But do not let it be that in restoring it, the fracture should become weaker And if it is restored alone, besides what is applied to the other limb, then it is necessary that the limb which has been closed should be opened always.
And know that the coxae is strung out in fifty days, or is added slightly, or diminished slightly: all this is according to the diversity of complexions and the remaining parts.
About the restoration of the fracture of the knee-joint
Know that the knee-joint rarely happens to fracture, but it often happens to it to be bruised many times
If it happens to fracture, then it is not without a break or a hole in its parts. It is just as with a wound, and without a wound And it remains in that state with sense.
And its restoration is in this way, that it be equal to what has been disrupted from its parts with the digits, until it is joined together again, and connected according to what is possible with equality and with facility, and with fabrication

About the restoration of the fracture of the thigh
Know that the thigh has two bones, of which one is thick, and is called the Cruris: and the other is thin, and is called the Focile
And it happens in both cases from the species of fracture which happens to two bones of the forearm: and because of this its restoration is effected, just as the restoration of the forearm equally, and the operation is one.
If two bones break together, the forearm turns around to all parts
If a thin bone breaks, the forearm turns around to the anterior part.
If a thick bone breaks, its lower part is found: and it is from that from which the bones do not appear.
Then administer extension, and equating, and ligation of the tendons according to that equally.
It is true that if the fracture of the forearm is a compound fracture, having many lacerations: so that the extension is less, and the alleviation is more gentle to you
And in the forearm there is something from the operation which is more than for the arm: and it is that when you equal the tendons and perform all your operation, then grasp two bands of pine wood which are administered in the fabrication of solar plates which are placed under the boards: and they become from the palm splints: and choose from those which have a small thickness, and neither are they from the smallest: and let their length be according to the length of the forearm from the knee to the lower part
Then bind over one and a half fingers two bands according to its length And put one part of the forearm from one side, and the other from the other side: and they are from the knee to the lower part of the foot
And bind two bands in three places from two extremities and middle When the tension is forbidden, the forearm declines to the right and left, and remains in a good position.
And thus is administered a cubulum of wood according to the length of the Cruris, and is placed in that Cruris, that it may preserve it from movement.
And much more is required, that you make it firm in fracture when it is its own bone
Then consider the Cruris every day, and study it with the utmost care from the apostemate, or swelling, or any other thing. If anything happens to it from these things, then oppose it with that which is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills it.
Of fractures of the bones of the Foot and Fingers.
The Calcaneus never fractures completely
The bones of the Foot indeed fracture sometimes when a fracture occurs, and the Fingers somewhat also: and it does not occur except by rubbing in various positions
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the Foot, and you wish to lift up that bone above itself, then place the patient's Foot upon the prepared Earth, like a walker
Then stand still, and place your Foot upon that which will lift up these bones: afterwards press, or apply a heel: and keep it equal, until it returns to its place
Then apply a compress, and bandage: and place under the Foot a board having two flat heads
Then bind the Foot with a strong bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloth and other things as necessary
When three or four days have passed, or four, then loosen the ligamentum You perceive the bones to be equal, whether they are fractured or displaced. The operation is thus in the bones, like water
If any fingers are fractured, then restore them, and make them equal as before, as I have said concerning the restoration of the fingers of the hand Then place your finger on the splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be broader than the other: then place under the hand this board which I have mentioned, and bind it securely.
If indeed that which is fractured from the bones is two, or three, or more, then place upon every finger a splint of its own quantity, bound in linen: and bind the board in the lower part of the foot: and let it rest upon one head of the board outside the foot, so that it may retain its stability
And it is convenient for you to remember, that you must consider all that I have said concerning the remaining fractures from accidents which we have mentioned: and oppose every accident with what is necessary.
Concerning the fracture of the woman's vulva, and the bone of the thigh, and the swelling of the man.
When the woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit in a square position. Then incline her to the side towards the back of her, and keep her firm behind
Then let the midwife grasp her vulva with her hand, until she grasps her: and make within the vulva like a sphere
Then let the woman groan, and raise her back gradually. The limb thus departs from the socket until it stands before the gate of Vulua like a sphere, and the fractured bone returns.
Then place a cushion over its back. When he wishes to move, move the limb easily, then mix: and reduce it as before, as you introduced it at the beginning: then return to its cushion first
Do this for seven days, or thereabouts, and the bone will be restored
And if necessary, take the urine from the privy, and apply it over the opening of the wound, and insert the whole bladder into the belly. Then blow into the bladder with urine, until the bladder is inflated inside the belly, the fracture will be restored.
Then keep it bound with the limb, and rest for a few days, as we have narrated, until it is healed.
When the bone of the breast of a man or a woman is fractured, then manage its restoration, and give it the same treatment as we have narrated for the bone of the elbow.
And do not hide the rectum in its near fractures, which are of little importance, especially for one who has studied or is skilled in this matter.
And understand my book well, for in many positions the fracture itself indicates to you the right way to restore and straighten it: understand therefore.

On the restoration of fractures of bones, when associated with a wound.
It is necessary in the restoration of fractures which are associated with a wound, especially if they are large, such as the femur or the pelvis, or similar to them, so that the wound is properly restored, and phlebotomy is performed immediately: if these conditions of phlebotomy are confirmed, as we have mentioned before.
If blood flows from the wound, it is necessary to ligate it immediately, so that the surrounding tissue is not powdered over the wound: if nothing else is present in the vicinity
Then commence the restoration of the fracture on the same day: do not delay it, if there is no swelling
If indeed swelling occurs, then defer the restoration until the ninth day, until the swelling has subsided Do not approach it in the third or fourth day, for you will cause him further injury
If, however, the fractured bone is prominent above the skin, you must reduce it and level it with your hands with ease and small extension
If it is not prepared for extension, and its equalization with your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, its length is equal to seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the size of the wound And it is necessary for the physician to take from him three, or four according to the quantity required for the cure of any kind of fracture
It must be round: and be of small size, so that it does not double under compression in the hour of operation: and it must have a sharp point at the extremities, which must have a margin at their extremities And above it to the breadth from the middle to the lower parts.
This is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that you place the acute edge of the instrument curved over the prominence of the bone: and push it with the same until the bone returns and is equal to the surrounding parts.
So to make the extremities of the fracture even with each other
If its extremities are slightly fractured and do not grasp the instrument well, then you must cut off the end of that bone until the instrument has the power to grasp it
If indeed it does not suffice to reduce them as we have said before, then cut it with the knife which we mentioned before: or draw out the bone with a saw in any way that is possible to you
Then remove what remains of the bone from the asperities and small fragments.
When it has been reduced and finds itself after reduction in pain and discomfort, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural position
Do this. For you must confer great comfort to the patient when you finish your restoration of the bone.
Then immerse the wound in vinegar, black. And if it is in summer. Do not place on the wound a wet cloth or anything containing oil: so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption
Then give the patient some urine at the end of your restoration of the bone Release the identified wound: thus, you shall cut with scissors the opening in the folds according to the quantity of the wound.

About the word 'Dislocation'.
Dislocation is the exit of a joint from its place Because it hinders its movement and deforms the limb's figure And causes the superimposition of excessive pain, and wounds that are harmful
When, therefore, any dislocation has happened, then it is necessary to hasten to restore it: neither must it be delayed at all
For if it is delayed, the place is left behind, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation itself
Therefore it is not permitted to move or extend it in the hour following the dislocation Because it makes many spasms and painful pains occur
But when it has happened, then it is necessary to prepare for a vein-cutting of the patient And afterwards let it be quieted until the swelling subsides
And then the limb is bound with warm water and oil Then it is reduced with ease.
And it heals every limb with that whose memory comes back to its place
I have indeed ordained the chapters of dislocation, also according to what has gone before in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to the lower part of it.
Concerning the care of dislocation of the lower jaw.</start_of_turn>
Rarely do two mandibles dislocate at the same time.</start_of_turn

On the reduction of the dislocation of the humerus
Know that the humerus is not displaced except in three ways.
The first is that it is displaced to the part of the elbow towards the lower parts. The second is that it is displaced towards the part of the chest The third is that it is displaced upwards of the humerus: and this happens rarely
And it does not slip backwards because of the sputum. And it does not slip backwards because of the nerves.
And it is mostly displaced and departs downwards towards the heel: especially in those in whom the flesh is little For it comes out quickly in some cases and enters quickly in others.
In those in whom the flesh is much, it is the opposite, that is, because it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty
And perhaps it happens to some people by a blow or an accident, and the Humerus is displaced by a hot abscess, and it is thought because of that that it is displaced
It is necessary therefore to experience this until you are certain of its extent, and then you can proceed with its treatment.
And it is recognized that it is displaced when it is downwards towards the heel: and you must distinguish between a displaced Humerus and a healthy Humerus. For you will find among them a clear difference, and you will find in the head of the Humerus a depression and under the heel of the Humerus almost at the touch of the hand as if it were a bone: and it is not possible to reduce the hand to its natural position, nor to move it in any way.
Similarly, if it is displaced towards the chest or upwards: then you will find a palpable mark, and it will not be hidden
And in these cases the displacement is easily reduced when it is recent, or when the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is as though you lift the hand up to the shoulder Then place your thumbs under the edge of the wound, and lift the suture upwards to its proper place: and the minister will elevate his hand and extend it upwards. Then place the lower hand downwards, and immediately return the hand to its place.

Then the patient lies down upon his back: and under the edge of the wound is placed a sphere and a bandage, which should be equal between softness and tension
Then the physician places his heel upon the sphere, and elevates the arm of the patient with force, and draws the patient's hand downwards.
And if desired, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you make the present man who is longer than the patient stand at the side, and let his arm pass under the wound of the patient: and elevate the edge of the wound upwards, until the patient is suspended in the air And another minister grasps the patient's hand downwards within his belly If, however, the patient is pale: then it is necessary, that he be suspended with something else, in order to lighten it: for the displacement returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that you fix in the ground a long stick, whose head is rounded like the sleeve of a coat of arms: which is neither thick nor thin Then is placed under the patient after those are placed over the head of the limb of water-linen: and the patient stands according to the length of the limb.
Then is extended his hand downwards from the other side, and his body is extended from the other side with force: for the suture returns to its place with speed.
Which if difficult be its reduction by all that we have said, then take care of it thus: and it is that you take a limb whose length be equal to the measure of two arms, and whose breadth be equal to the measure of four fingers, and whose thickness be equal to the measure of two fingers: which has a round head, that it may alleviate its entrance into depth of the wound, according to this form
Then are bound over the round head of the limb water-linen, that it do not grieve the limb of the patient
Then is placed under the wound of the patient, that the hand or arm be extended over the limb: and the limb is bound over the adjunct and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then is placed the arm over the degree of the scale for the width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is let down from the other side The suture enters immediately.
When it is thus completed the entrance of it according to whatever manner is possible: then it is necessary, that you place under the wound a sphere equal in quantity of wool. Then place a prepared dressing of ground chaff with olive oil and albumin over the whole arm, as is usual above
Then bind under the wound a decent suture: and turn the ligament over the dressing above: and connect the hand to its neck, and let go, neither move the hand for seven days.
It is necessary that food be placed for the sick person until the limb is comfortable: for thus is it quicker to heal.
Then food is placed after seven days, or five, and is discontinued when movement returns. If it remains firm and is not softened, then it is healed
If the limb is dislocated many times, because of the humidity which has occurred to it, or for another cause: then it is necessary that an incision be made with three holes, according to what is stated in the chapter on cautery
If this is done completely, and the ligament is dissolved after seven days, and the dislocation does not persist, and the swelling and tension above the limb are reduced, and does not firm up, and falls away, and does not lift up the limb towards the shoulder: then you know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus are either cut, or stretched, or softened: and you know that the dislocation will never take hold in its place
A dislocation which occurs towards the breast and the armpit, or towards the back, is reduced with a push and an extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining tension and care are administered until it is healed
If after healing there is still greatness in the limb, and tardiness in its movement: then the sick person is to be bathed many times, until the greatness is softened, and it returns to its first nature.

You know that the dislocation of the Cubiti is difficult, and returns with difficulty also: and it dislocates towards all parts, and especially towards the front, or towards the back.
And its displacement is not concealed: because it hangs down under the skin and under the touch, according to whatever figure it is displaced. And when you join the displaced limb with the sound one, it is declared that it is evident to you The limb is united and cannot be doubled, neither can it touch with its own shoulder
And it is necessary to take care of it promptly, and reduce the displacement immediately, before a callous swelling occurs.
For if a callous swelling should occur, its reduction will be difficult: and it may not be healed at all, especially if it is at the back In every kind of displacement there is pain: and it increases with swelling: and it often descends with it.
And the restoration of it when it is possible to reduce it, is that you extend the limb with two hands: and the limb is expanded, and the hands of the physician are above the elbow and below it, and he impels the union with his thumbs together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place
Or else the displacement is to the front: then it returns like this, that you double the hand with the limb, until it touches with the root of its palm the displaced elbow
If the displacement does not obey reduction, then manage the extension vigorously: and keep it firm: that is, extend the limb with two ministers: and hold it with two ministers also, so that it does not move during extension.
And then the limb is turned towards all parts after it is covered over with the doubled long panus or the broad ligament
And when the physician touches the limb, let him smear it with oil: so that it is lubricated with the things that aid in the smoothness of the joint Then it drives the suture forward with tension until it returns.
After its reduction it is necessary to place above it the dressing which has been applied, in which there is styptic material, and which should be dried with alum. And it should be sutured securely. The arm should be suspended from the collar of the patient: and it should be allowed to rest for a few days.
Then it should be dissolved. If, however, the suture remains fixed in its place, then the ligament should be severed from it, and the latter should be discarded.
And if the suture has not been properly secured, then repeat the dressing, and the ligament and discard it for a few days until it is secured Then dissolve it
If, however, there should be pain after the reduction of the suture, and the arrangement should be moving: then apply moisture to the bath, and gentle friction, and compression, until the pain subsides, or put above the suture some lint, that is, wool fat And it should be bound, and be allowed to rest above the dressing day and night. Then remove the dressing, and put it into a bath When it is wet, then gently friction the suture And repeat over it three times with warm oil, once, twice, and three times with the introduction of the bath, until it is softened
And if desired, put upon it soft wool fat, wet, warmed with butter, and bind over it.
Do this every day She herself is softened, and returns to her own state.

If, however, its movement is obstructed, and something heavy has fallen into it: then soften it with warm water and gentle friction, until it becomes soft.
In case of dislocation of the fingers.
When the finger is dislocated from all its parts, or from the palm of the hand, or from the wrist: then extend the finger and manipulate the dislocation with your thumbs until it is reduced
Then support the finger and suspend it with a bandage over the part which is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is restored to its proper position on that day
When night has fallen, bind it according to the instructions given: and do not cease to loosen it daily: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for a few days, until it is healed
And do the same in the case of dislocation of the wrist: and bind it to the part itself, and do the same with your first operation, until it is healed
And do the same in the case of dislocation of the other parts
Chapter 31. In case of dislocation of the shoulder joint
When something has happened to the shoulder joint, or a complete dislocation of the collarbone, or many bones of the shoulder have been displaced from their places: then it is not in this case that treatment is necessary, because the poison is harmful to the sick person.
And the sign of this is that the sick person departs without wishing to stay, and is unable to keep still: and they soften various parts of it, or two of its limbs, or two of its arms, or one of them: or they displace one of the bones from its place
Many are the things that are moved, because many are their removals to four parts.
Many are the things that are moved, because many of their removals are to the posterior, and are called Gibbositas.
The care of the latter is such, that if the Gibbositas arise from infancy, it is not in that care, neither is there any healing from it
However, in those things which arise from an accident, or from a blow, or from things like these, as the ancients said in a long discourse, their methods of treatment are old.</start_of_turn> Most of them do not return when proficiency is restored.</start_of_turn>
I myself have abridged from this, because of the proximity of my purpose to it: and the manner in which I have exposed it, and the form of my instruments differs from that which they have shown.
I say, therefore, that in the Gibbositas which arise from the anterior part, there is neither ingenuity nor healing from it.</start_of_turn>
And in like manner in those things which are done to the two sides also.</start_of_turn
And it is not cured except for that which happens in the proper back, as I have said: and that is, when the sickly person is stretched out over his face over a level base near the wall: and when there is stretched out under that a moist instrument, so that his chest is not bruised.</start_of_turn
Then a straight stick is placed fixed in the fire in the ground, at the foot of it, round about the head: and another stick is placed round about the feet at the other foot: and the minister holds the stick, and it must not be fixed in the fire.</start_of_turn And the other attendant holds the other one.
Then hold the head of the patient and soften the ligament under the cheekbones: and stretch out the end of the ligament to the bone that is near his head: and bind it there.
Then bind another ligament above the armpits, above the knees and at his buttocks Then gather all the ligaments and bind them to the other leg which is at his feet
Then stretch out all the attendants at the parts of the ligaments, and do not move away two bones from the places in which they are fixed: indeed they should not be fixed too tightly, as we said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength, until he recovers: or places them on a board. Then he binds his feet to the board until he recovers.
If, however, he does not recover by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms And put the heel of the board against the wall from which we said that it is near the patient, the place where the extremity of the board will be placed Then place the middle of the board on the spine. And the doctor places his feet on the other extremity And bind well, until the vertebra are firm and recover to their place.
And if you wish, work with the ankle which is involved with your hand: and it is that two bones, whose length is that of an arm, are placed beneath the head of the patient at the end of two toes: and two bones at the head of him at the end of two toes.
And let the length be that which is between every two palms of the hand: and it is made in every place of the hole through which the [blood] flows, and it closes every wood in the earth much, so that it does not move at all.
And the rounded wood is introduced: and the [ligament] is turned in the hole through which it returns between two holes of two woods.
And at its extremity is a hole in which the wood is closed, whose length is a palm with that which is turned: and in two other woods it is like this
Then string the ligaments which you bound in the chest of the patient around the turn which is at his head: and the ligaments which you bound in his thighs around the turn which is at his feet.
Then stand at every turn of the minister who twists his hand with a bandage, with which the turn is turned: and the doctor equalizes the Spondile, as we have said
This is the form of the turn which follows.
Then when the Spondile returns, and the place is equal, then it is necessary to put on an exicated plaster with albumin or oil Then rest. Then put above the plaster a star made of a table, whose width is three fingers, or somewhat less And its length is the quantity which covers the place of the elbow, and over the sound part of the Spondile
And it is bound with the ligament which we mentioned before. And the patient should be fed with delicate food until he is healed
If anything remains prominent in the place at the end of the healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines which soften and soothe, with the administration of the plaster as we have said in the long term And it is administered in that layer of lead.
And when an eminence appears at the last vertebra of the spine: it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is the bone which is now emining.</start_of_turn> Therefore it is not appropriate to make an assumption about it with this medicine: perhaps because of that it will cause death.</start_of_turn>
About the treatment of a dislocated ankle.</start_of_turn
You know that the dislocations of the ankle and the humerus do not happen except for a complete dislocation: neither do they happen except for a slight displacement and a concave curvature.</start_of_turn
And the dislocation of the ankle is divided into four parts.</start_of_turn That is, because it dislocates forward and backward.</start_of_turn
And most of them displace inward: and little of them displace forward and backward.</start_of_turn
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is thus: because when you join the sound limb with the diseased limb, it is longer: and the limb which is diseased is more prominent than the sound limb, and it cannot double up its foot beside the ankle: and the place which follows the ankle after the heel is called the heel-bone, because the head of the fibula lies there.</start_of_turn
And the sign of its outward dislocation is that there are signs of its opposite on these signs.</start_of_turn
And the sign of its forward dislocation is that it expands the sound limb according to its complement: but it does not double itself without pain in the knee: and if it attempts to walk, it cannot go beyond that forward: and it retains its urine, and its ankle is swollen, and there is a tingling sensation in the walk.</start_of_turn
And the sign of it [dislocation] to which it has happened is that the knee does not expand and cannot duplicate itself before it has doubled the knee: and its thigh is also shorter on the other side: and the knee is softened, and the head of the coxae is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this dislocation is of the species which conserves the limb as a whole, if the dislocation is old, which has existed since its origin with the limb itself Then if you perform the reduction of it, and it remains as before: then there is no healing in that at all It is not right that a suspicion should be formed of it.
In that case in which the dislocation is fixed, and is of the species of the four dislocations: then it is proper for you to join them together, and to extend the limb inwards and outwards, and to move the limb from side to side: perhaps it will return by itself, and no other cure will be needed.
If it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong minister, and to extend the thigh from the lower part: either with the hands or with a ligature which is tied above the knee: and another minister extends the hand above, so that it inserts the hand under the toes. Then tighten with a ligament loosely over the root of the coxae, and keep the extremity of the other ligament.
And let the extension be from the anterior part of the knee to the part of the ischium, or from the posterior part to the part of the back
And let the extension be throughout in one way, until the patient lifts himself with his body from the earth, and remains hanging
And this is the common species of the four species of extension.
If, therefore, the displacement returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every kind to perform that which I shall say concerning the proper care.
When reduction has been performed within, the patient must rest on the sound side over the painful area Then the ligament is placed over the root of the thigh, in that place where the head of the thigh is, and the place under the groin Then the ligament is extended from the groin upwards to the upper part of the body, to the part of the breastbone.
Then another strong hand takes hold of the limb, and grasps the great toe of the lower limb, and extends it outwards to its full extent. And thus it returns to its place
And this kind is easier than the rest of the methods for reducing this limb.
If, however, you are excused by him, and he does not obey your order with this method: then it is necessary to bind the two feet of the patient together with a strong, soft ligament, over the two heels and over the two knees: and let the length of each be equal to the quantity of four fingers: and let the limb be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And the patient is suspended over the head of the limb: and let the length of it from the ground be equal to the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong youth to grasp the head of the thigh: and suspend him with the patient by another: and the youth will drive out the thigh with force, for the join will return to its place with speed.
When the dislocation is external, the patient must lie upon a board as we have mentioned in the case of gibbosity. And the ligament must be tied over the limb itself and over the abdomen
Then two boards are placed, one under the feet and the other under the head of the limb itself. And a board is placed in the middle of the foot, fixed firmly: upon which are placed moist cloths, so that it does not cause pain to the patient: and let the board be between the thighs, so that it does not draw towards the lower parts at the extension.
And extend all the limbs to their proper place, and the physician should equal the reduction with his hand.
If this be done, it will be good.
And if not, place upon the limb itself a board, and press upon it, as we said in the case of gibbosity, equally. But it must be that the patient lies upon his sound side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then it must be that the limb of the patient be extended quickly: and this is according to the same narration as upon the board: and the physician puts his right hand upon the patient's knee And then grasp it with the other hand, and with this coaction put it extended towards the lower part towards the knee
And if the dislocation is backward, then it must not be that the patient be extended downwards, but let him be elevated above the ground: indeed it must be that he be placed upon a hard object, as it must be that it be done even if the dislocation is wide towards the exterior, as we said concerning the recumbent position: and it is upon his face, and the ligaments are extended: according to what we said above
It is necessary that compression be administered with a board also above the place where the joint protrudes, when reduction is completed according to what is required.
The sign of its reduction is not hidden by the board: when you extend the two limbs of the patient, and when you have made them equal in length, and the patient tightens his limb and expands it without straining: then you know that the limb is returned according to what is required. Then join the two thighs together and apply a compress, and bind securely, that is, with a rotation, so that it does not move to any part: and the patient should rest quietly for three days, or four.
Then loosen the ligament and the compress, and compare the limb with the other: when you find them equal in length, then you know that the dislocation is finally stabilized: absolutely so, the patient can walk again.
If you see any softening: then reduce it again, and apply the compress once more, and bind it securely as before: and discharge it also after three days.
Then loosen it and make it walk slowly on both sides for a few days, until it is comfortable again.

The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is, posteriorly
It is not dislocated forwards
The sign of its dislocation is that the patient pulls his limb towards his thigh. If it does not adhere to the thigh: then you know that the knee is dislocated
And the restoration of all these displacements is that you should make the sick man sit with his right foot extended over his left foot, and that you should place his hand upon his face.
Then adhere his hand to your palms and stretch his leg between your fingers over his knee. Then place his hand with both palms against his side with force. And another attendant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of recovery is that the leg adheres to the thigh gently, not forcefully.
Then bandage it, and adhere the leg to the thigh. Then bind it with three or four bands. Then finish with it.
And do not allow the patient to walk for a few days until he is healed.</start_of_turn>
If, however, he resists the reduction, then manage the extension strongly with bands, as was mentioned in the treatment of the ankle, until it returns.</start_of_turn>
Regarding the treatment of ankle dislocation.</start_of_turn>
The calcaneus is sometimes dislocated with a small displacement, and sometimes with a greater displacement.</start_of_turn And the dislocation can occur either inwards or outwards.</start_of_turn
And the sign of its dislocation is that the calcaneus is swollen, proceeding to the part towards which it is displaced.</start_of_turn
The treatment for its removal is easy reduction: it is to extend easily with the hands and restore it, until it returns.</start_of_turn
When the rectum is dislocated according to the complement, it is necessary to make it sit upright: and keep the minister strong at the back of its dorsi, in the middle of it.
Hold it with your right hand at its upper part and with your left hand at its lower part at the heel of the foot
Then draw the foot towards you with your right hand. And with your left hand support the two heels.
Then stretch it out with your left hand, and push the heel of the foot with your right hand without straining
Do this twice as we have said.
Then push the heel of the foot to the knee in the third part, and you draw the heel.
If it returns to its place once, or twice in this way, and you have equalized the foot, that is good: and if not, do the operation again over it: because it will return
If, however, it is forbidden for you to reduce it as we have said: then make it lie on its back on the earth over the earth, and fix the heel firmly in the earth on the side, so that it rests between its thighs: and put your hand over it so that it does not cause pain to the patient
Then take hold of the hip: afterwards extend the other limb of the foot, or with your hands, or with the ligament which is over the ankle of the foot. Then extend all the limbs against the extension of each other: and the heel should be stable between the two thighs, holding it so that it does not draw the body downwards towards the extension.
Then the doctor should equalize the dislocation with his hands, and the other limb should keep the knee of the sound limb towards the lower parts The dislocation returns with speed.
When the dislocation returns, and its soundness is declared: then apply an ointment and a compress, and bind it firmly with ligaments, and bind the limb with a ligament to the thigh.
It is necessary to beware of nerves, which are above the heel behind, so that there is no tension on the ligaments over them, and that it does not damage them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the ligament is softened, bind it: then loosen it on the third, or fourth day: and prevent the patient from walking for forty days. If he attempts to walk before that space of time, there will be no security that the dislocation will be dissolved and not cause corruption, and will not recover after that treatment
If there should happen to him a soft swelling: then it is necessary, that you give him rest in that place in which our story proceeds elsewhere in the treatment, and embrocation, until it is moved out
Regarding the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that it be equalized, that which is dislocated from the limb with little extension without work: and that indeed is not difficult, rather is easy
If however the dislocation is in some part of the heel of the foot: then it is necessary that you make the patient sit down and rest his foot on the same level as the earth, or on a board: and he is as if walking.
Then sit down, and place your foot on that which proceeds from those joints. Then let you support that with your foot until it returns, and keep it even, so that it does not appear in a prominent place.
Then put under the foot a board that will embrace the whole foot, which has two heads. Then bind it with a bandage for three days. Then heal it, and keep it still from walking for many days, until it is bound up and protected from reduction
As for the kinds of dislocations with wound or fracture
When anything of this kind happens, and you know its cure and restoration, many things follow after: and for this reason it is not fitting to be proud of its cure, which is dangerous if you are not learned in the art of long practice, merciful, pious, prompt, not cowardly, nor presumptuous: and that you should submit to the judgment of the physicians who sit in the warm waiting-room. Unless you know, or expect salvation from the mercy of gentleness with regard to the pain, and in which something appears promising. Attempt to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the case, before the swelling becomes cold.
If the limb returns, as we wish, then administer a regimen that will reduce the swelling: and care for the wound with the things that suit it from the drying unguents
If the dislocation is with fracture, and the bones are separated in the sitting position, then attempt to break them off, and do in that what we said in the ailments of simple fractures in the places of their fracture
And be careful according to your skill, and guard yourself from the entrance of fear: according to what has been said to you before
It is better for you to be pleasing to your Creator, and to persevere in his glory, and to have health for your body in this life.
